## Chapter: Nonlinear Wave Theory

### Introduction

The study of waves is a fundamental aspect of oceanography, and it is a critical component in understanding the behavior of ships and offshore energy systems. In the previous chapters, we have explored the linear wave theory, which provides a simplified understanding of wave propagation. However, the real world is inherently nonlinear, and many phenomena in the ocean cannot be accurately described using linear wave theory. This is where nonlinear wave theory comes into play.

In this chapter, we will delve into the nonlinear wave theory, a more complex but more accurate model of wave propagation. Nonlinear wave theory takes into account the nonlinear effects that are present in the ocean, such as wave-wave interactions and wave-current interactions. These effects can significantly alter the behavior of waves, and understanding them is crucial for predicting and mitigating the effects of waves on ships and offshore energy systems.

We will begin by introducing the basic concepts of nonlinear wave theory, such as the nonlinear wave equation and the concept of wave-wave interactions. We will then explore the implications of these concepts for the behavior of waves in the ocean. We will also discuss the methods used to solve the nonlinear wave equation, including numerical methods and perturbation methods.

Finally, we will discuss the applications of nonlinear wave theory in the field of oceanography. This includes the prediction of rogue waves, the study of wave-induced forces on ships and offshore structures, and the design of offshore energy systems that can withstand the effects of waves.

This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive introduction to nonlinear wave theory, suitable for both students and professionals in the field of oceanography. By the end of this chapter, readers should have a solid understanding of the principles of nonlinear wave theory and its applications in the study of waves in the ocean.




#### 2.3b Wave Transmission Mechanisms

Wave transmission is a fundamental aspect of wave propagation in the ocean. It occurs when a wave encounters a boundary between two different media, such as water and air, and part of the wave is transmitted into the second medium. The second-order wave theory provides a mathematical framework for studying wave transmission.

The second-order wave theory is used to derive the wave transmission equations, which describe the propagation of transmitted waves in the ocean. These equations are given by:

$$
\frac{\partial \eta}{\partial t} + c\frac{\partial \eta}{\partial x} + \frac{1}{2}g\frac{\partial \eta^2}{\partial x} = 0
$$

$$
\frac{\partial \eta}{\partial x} + \frac{1}{c}\frac{\partial \eta}{\partial t} + \frac{1}{2}g\frac{\partial \eta^2}{\partial x} = 0
$$

where $\eta$ is the wave amplitude, $t$ is time, $x$ is the spatial coordinate, $c$ is the wave speed, and $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity. These equations describe the propagation of transmitted waves in the ocean.

The transmission coefficient, denoted by $T$, is a key parameter in wave transmission. It is defined as the ratio of the transmitted wave amplitude to the incident wave amplitude. The transmission coefficient can be calculated using the continuity condition at the boundary, which states that the sum of the incident wave and the reflected wave must equal the transmitted wave.

The transmission coefficient can be expressed in terms of the wave speeds in the two media as follows:

$$
T = \left(\frac{c_2 + c_1}{c_2 - c_1}\right)^2
$$

where $c_1$ and $c_2$ are the wave speeds in the first and second medium, respectively. This equation shows that the transmission coefficient is dependent on the difference in wave speeds between the two media.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of wave reflection and transmission in the context of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems.

#### 2.3c Wave Reflection and Transmission Coefficients

The reflection and transmission coefficients are crucial parameters in the study of wave propagation in the ocean. They provide a quantitative measure of the amount of wave energy that is reflected and transmitted at the boundary between two different media. 

The reflection coefficient, denoted by $R$, is defined as the ratio of the reflected wave amplitude to the incident wave amplitude. It can be calculated using the continuity condition at the boundary, which states that the sum of the incident wave and the reflected wave must equal the transmitted wave. The reflection coefficient can be expressed in terms of the wave speeds in the two media as follows:

$$
R = \left(\frac{c_2 - c_1}{c_2 + c_1}\right)^2
$$

where $c_1$ and $c_2$ are the wave speeds in the first and second medium, respectively. This equation shows that the reflection coefficient is dependent on the difference in wave speeds between the two media.

The transmission coefficient, denoted by $T$, is defined as the ratio of the transmitted wave amplitude to the incident wave amplitude. It can also be calculated using the continuity condition at the boundary. The transmission coefficient can be expressed in terms of the wave speeds in the two media as follows:

$$
T = \left(\frac{c_2 + c_1}{c_2 - c_1}\right)^2
$$

where $c_1$ and $c_2$ are the wave speeds in the first and second medium, respectively. This equation shows that the transmission coefficient is also dependent on the difference in wave speeds between the two media.

The reflection and transmission coefficients are complementary, meaning that their sum is always equal to 1. This is a direct consequence of the conservation of wave energy at the boundary between two media. 

In the context of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems, the reflection and transmission coefficients play a crucial role in determining the amount of wave energy that is absorbed by the system. This is particularly important for the design and operation of offshore energy systems, where the absorption of wave energy can lead to fatigue damage and structural failure.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of wave reflection and transmission in the context of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems.




#### 2.3c Wave Reflection and Transmission Coefficients

The reflection and transmission coefficients are fundamental parameters in the study of wave propagation in the ocean. They describe the behavior of waves when encountering a boundary between two different media, such as water and air. 

The reflection coefficient, denoted by $R$, is defined as the ratio of the reflected wave amplitude to the incident wave amplitude. It can be calculated using the continuity condition at the boundary, which states that the sum of the incident wave and the reflected wave must equal the transmitted wave. The reflection coefficient can be expressed in terms of the wave speeds in the two media as follows:

$$
R = \left(\frac{c_1 - c_2}{c_1 + c_2}\right)^2
$$

where $c_1$ and $c_2$ are the wave speeds in the first and second medium, respectively. This equation shows that the reflection coefficient is dependent on the difference in wave speeds between the two media.

The transmission coefficient, denoted by $T$, was discussed in the previous section. It is defined as the ratio of the transmitted wave amplitude to the incident wave amplitude. The transmission coefficient can be calculated using the continuity condition at the boundary, which states that the sum of the incident wave and the reflected wave must equal the transmitted wave. The transmission coefficient can be expressed in terms of the wave speeds in the two media as follows:

$$
T = \left(\frac{c_2 + c_1}{c_2 - c_1}\right)^2
$$

where $c_1$ and $c_2$ are the wave speeds in the first and second medium, respectively. This equation shows that the transmission coefficient is dependent on the difference in wave speeds between the two media.

The reflection and transmission coefficients are complementary, meaning that their sum is always equal to 1. This is a direct consequence of the continuity condition at the boundary. 

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of wave reflection and transmission in the context of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems.




#### 2.4a Definition of Wave Dispersion

Wave dispersion is a fundamental concept in the study of wave propagation in the ocean. It refers to the phenomenon where waves of different wavelengths travel at different speeds. This is in contrast to non-dispersive waves, where all frequencies travel at the same speed. 

In the context of ocean waves, dispersion can be caused by several factors, including the depth of the water, the presence of currents, and the properties of the water itself. For instance, in deep water, longer waves tend to travel faster than shorter waves. This is due to the fact that longer waves have a larger wavelength, which allows them to travel faster. 

Dispersion can also be caused by the presence of currents. If a wave is traveling in a direction that is not parallel to the current, the wave will be slowed down by the current. This is because the wave is constantly being pushed sideways by the current, which causes it to lose energy and speed. 

The properties of the water can also cause dispersion. For example, the presence of salt in the water can affect the speed of waves. Salt water is denser than fresh water, which means that waves traveling in salt water will travel faster than waves traveling in fresh water. 

The concept of dispersion is closely related to the concept of wave velocity. The wave velocity is a measure of how fast a wave is traveling. For dispersive waves, the wave velocity is dependent on the wavelength of the wave. This is in contrast to non-dispersive waves, where the wave velocity is constant for all wavelengths.

The wave velocity can be calculated using the dispersion relationship, which relates the angular frequency of the wave to its wavenumber. For dispersive waves, the dispersion relationship is a function of the wavenumber, meaning that the wave velocity is dependent on the wavelength of the wave. 

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of wave dispersion in more detail, including its effects on wave propagation and its implications for ship and offshore energy systems.

#### 2.4b Wave Dispersion in Deep Water

In deep water, the dispersion of waves is primarily governed by the depth of the water and the wavelength of the waves. The dispersion relationship in deep water can be approximated by the following equation:

$$
\omega = \sqrt{gk}
$$

where $\omega$ is the angular frequency, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, and $k$ is the wavenumber. This equation shows that the phase speed of the waves, which is the speed at which the phase of the wave propagates, is proportional to the square root of the wavenumber. 

The wavenumber, in turn, is inversely proportional to the wavelength. This means that longer waves, which have a larger wavelength, will travel faster than shorter waves. This is the primary mechanism of wave dispersion in deep water.

The dispersion relationship in deep water can also be expressed in terms of the wave frequency and the water depth. The wave frequency, $f$, is related to the wave speed, $c$, and the water depth, $h$, by the following equation:

$$
f = \frac{c}{2h}
$$

This equation shows that the wave frequency is inversely proportional to the water depth. This means that waves with a higher frequency, which correspond to shorter wavelengths, will travel faster than waves with a lower frequency.

The dispersion of waves in deep water can have significant implications for ship and offshore energy systems. For instance, the dispersion of waves can affect the design of offshore structures, as different frequencies of waves will interact with the structure in different ways. Similarly, the dispersion of waves can affect the design of ships, as different frequencies of waves will interact with the ship in different ways.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of wave dispersion in shallow water, where the dispersion of waves is primarily governed by the presence of the seafloor.

#### 2.4c Wave Dispersion in Shallow Water

In shallow water, the dispersion of waves is primarily governed by the presence of the seafloor and the wavelength of the waves. The dispersion relationship in shallow water can be approximated by the following equation:

$$
\omega = \sqrt{gk + k^2h}
$$

where $\omega$ is the angular frequency, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, $k$ is the wavenumber, and $h$ is the water depth. This equation shows that the phase speed of the waves, which is the speed at which the phase of the wave propagates, is proportional to the square root of the sum of the squares of the wavenumber and the wavenumber times the water depth.

The wavenumber, in turn, is inversely proportional to the wavelength. This means that longer waves, which have a larger wavelength, will travel faster than shorter waves. However, in shallow water, the presence of the seafloor can cause the dispersion of waves to deviate from the simple relationship observed in deep water.

The dispersion relationship in shallow water can also be expressed in terms of the wave frequency and the water depth. The wave frequency, $f$, is related to the wave speed, $c$, and the water depth, $h$, by the following equation:

$$
f = \frac{c}{2h}
$$

This equation shows that the wave frequency is inversely proportional to the water depth. This means that waves with a higher frequency, which correspond to shorter wavelengths, will travel faster than waves with a lower frequency. However, the presence of the seafloor can cause the wave frequency to deviate from this simple relationship.

The dispersion of waves in shallow water can have significant implications for ship and offshore energy systems. For instance, the dispersion of waves can affect the design of offshore structures, as different frequencies of waves will interact with the structure in different ways. Similarly, the dispersion of waves can affect the design of ships, as different frequencies of waves will interact with the ship in different ways.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of wave dispersion in the presence of currents, which can further complicate the dispersion of waves in the ocean.




#### 2.4b Dispersion Relations

The dispersion relation is a fundamental concept in wave theory that describes the relationship between the angular frequency of a wave and its wavenumber. For dispersive waves, the dispersion relation is a function of the wavenumber, meaning that the wave velocity is dependent on the wavelength of the wave. This is in contrast to non-dispersive waves, where the wave velocity is constant for all wavelengths.

The dispersion relation can be expressed mathematically as:

$$
\omega = \omega(k)
$$

where $\omega$ is the angular frequency and $k$ is the wavenumber. The dispersion relation is a key tool in understanding the behavior of waves in the ocean, as it allows us to predict how waves will interact with different mediums and structures.

In the context of ocean waves, the dispersion relation can be used to calculate the wave velocity as a function of the wavelength. This is particularly useful in the study of wave dispersion, as it allows us to understand how waves of different wavelengths will travel at different speeds.

The dispersion relation can also be used to understand the phenomenon of wave steepening. As waves propagate, they tend to steepen, which can lead to the formation of shock waves. The dispersion relation can be used to calculate the rate of wave steepening, which is a key factor in understanding the behavior of waves in the ocean.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the concept of wave dispersion and explore its implications for ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems.

#### 2.4c Wave Dispersion in Shallow Water

In the previous sections, we have discussed the concept of wave dispersion and its importance in understanding the behavior of waves in the ocean. In this section, we will focus on the dispersion of waves in shallow water.

Shallow water refers to a water body where the water depth is much smaller than the wavelength of the wave. In such cases, the wave propagation is significantly affected by the bottom topography and the wave steepening phenomenon becomes more pronounced.

The dispersion relation for shallow water waves can be expressed as:

$$
\omega = \sqrt{gk}
$$

where $\omega$ is the angular frequency, $k$ is the wavenumber, and $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity. This equation shows that the wave velocity is proportional to the square root of the wavenumber, which means that waves with larger wavelengths will travel faster than waves with smaller wavelengths.

The dispersion relation for shallow water waves can also be used to understand the phenomenon of wave breaking. As waves propagate in shallow water, they tend to steepen and eventually break, leading to the generation of turbulence and mixing of the water column. The dispersion relation can be used to calculate the wave breaking height, which is a key factor in understanding the behavior of waves in the ocean.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of wave breaking in more detail and discuss its implications for ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems.

\omega = \sqrt{gk}

As waves propagate in subtropical waters, they tend to steepen and eventually break, leading to the generation of turbulence and mixing of the water column. The dispersion relation can be used to calculate the wave breaking height, which is a key factor in understanding the behavior of waves in the ocean.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of wave breaking in more detail and discuss its implications for ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems.

#### 2.4p Wave Dispersion in Subtropical Waters

In the previous sections, we have discussed the dispersion of waves in shallow, deep, coastal, offshore, harbor, estuarine, lake, river, polar, tropical, temperate, and subpolar waters. In this section, we will focus on the dispersion of waves in subtropical waters.

Subtropical waters refer to the region near the equator, but outside the tropics. This region is characterized by warm temperatures and minimal ice cover. The dispersion of waves in subtropical waters is influenced by the deep water dispersion relation, as the water depths in this region are typically much larger than the wavelengths of the waves.

The dispersion relation for subtropical waters can be expressed as:

$$
\omega = \sqrt{gk}
$$

where $\omega$ is the angular frequency, $k$ is the wavenumber, and $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity. This equation shows that the wave velocity is proportional to the square root of the wavenumber, which means that waves with larger wavelengths will travel faster than waves with smaller wavelengths.

The dispersion relation for subtropical waters can also be used to understand the phenomenon of wave breaking. As waves propagate in subtropical waters, they tend to steepen and eventually break, leading to the generation of turbulence and mixing of the water column. The dispersion relation can be used to calculate the wave breaking height, which is a key factor in understanding the behavior of waves in the ocean.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of wave breaking in more detail and discuss its implications for ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems.

#### 2.4c Wave Dispersion in Shallow Water

In the previous sections, we have discussed the concept of wave dispersion and its importance in understanding the behavior of waves in the ocean. In this section, we will focus on the dispersion of waves in shallow water.

Shallow water refers to a water body where the water depth is much smaller than the wavelength of the wave. In such cases, the wave propagation is significantly affected by the water depth, leading to wave dispersion. The dispersion of waves in shallow water is particularly important in the study of ocean waves, as it affects the propagation of waves towards the shore.

The dispersion relation for waves in shallow water can be expressed as:

$$
\omega = \sqrt{gk}
$$

where $\omega$ is the angular frequency, $k$ is the wavenumber, and $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity. This relation shows that the wave velocity is dependent on the wavelength, with longer waves traveling faster than shorter waves. This is a direct result of the wave dispersion in shallow water.

The dispersion of waves in shallow water can also be visualized using the concept of wave steepening. As waves propagate in shallow water, they tend to steepen, leading to the formation of shock waves. The dispersion relation can be used to calculate the rate of wave steepening, which is a key factor in understanding the behavior of waves in the ocean.

In the next section, we will explore the implications of wave dispersion in shallow water for ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems.

We have learned that linear wave theory is based on the assumption that the wave amplitude is small compared to the water depth, while second-order wave theory takes into account the nonlinear effects of wave propagation. These theories are essential for understanding the behavior of ocean waves and their interaction with ships and offshore energy systems.

We have also discussed the mathematical models used in linear and second-order wave theory, including the linear wave equation and the nonlinear wave equation. These equations allow us to describe the propagation of ocean waves and their effects on ships and offshore energy systems. By solving these equations, we can gain valuable insights into the behavior of ocean waves and their impact on various systems.

Furthermore, we have explored the applications of linear and second-order wave theory in the field of ocean engineering. These theories are crucial for designing and analyzing ships and offshore energy systems, as they provide a fundamental understanding of the wave behavior and its effects. By applying these theories, engineers can ensure the safety and reliability of these systems in the harsh marine environment.

In conclusion, linear and second-order wave theory are essential tools for understanding the behavior of ocean waves and their interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. By studying these theories, we can gain a deeper understanding of the complex dynamics of the ocean and its impact on various systems. This knowledge is crucial for the design, analysis, and operation of ships and offshore energy systems in the ever-changing ocean environment.

## Chapter 3: Wave Propagation:




### Introduction

### Section: 3.1 Definition of Regular Waves:

Regular waves, also known as progressive waves, are a type of wave that propagate through a medium without changing their shape or speed. They are characterized by their uniform motion and constant amplitude, making them ideal for studying the basic principles of wave propagation and interaction.

#### 3.1a Characteristics of Regular Waves

Regular waves have several key characteristics that distinguish them from other types of waves. These include:

1. **Uniform motion:** Regular waves move through a medium with a constant velocity. This means that the wave crests and troughs move at the same speed, maintaining a constant distance between them.

2. **Constant amplitude:** The amplitude of regular waves remains constant as they propagate. This means that the height of the wave crests and depth of the wave troughs do not change over time.

3. **Linear polarization:** Regular waves are linearly polarized, meaning that the electric field vector of the wave oscillates in a single plane. This is in contrast to circularly or elliptically polarized waves, which have electric field vectors that rotate in a circular or elliptical path.

4. **Constant wavelength:** The wavelength of regular waves remains constant as they propagate. This means that the distance between successive wave crests or troughs does not change over time.

5. **Constant frequency:** The frequency of regular waves remains constant as they propagate. This means that the number of wave cycles that pass a given point in a given time does not change.

These characteristics make regular waves ideal for studying the basic principles of wave propagation and interaction. They also make them a fundamental concept in the study of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems.

In the next section, we will explore the mathematical representation of regular waves, using the popular Markdown format and the MathJax library to render equations in TeX and LaTeX style syntax. This will allow us to express complex mathematical concepts in a clear and concise manner.




### Section: 3.1 Definition of Regular Waves:

Regular waves, also known as progressive waves, are a type of wave that propagate through a medium without changing their shape or speed. They are characterized by their uniform motion and constant amplitude, making them ideal for studying the basic principles of wave propagation and interaction.

#### 3.1a Characteristics of Regular Waves

Regular waves have several key characteristics that distinguish them from other types of waves. These include:

1. **Uniform motion:** Regular waves move through a medium with a constant velocity. This means that the wave crests and troughs move at the same speed, maintaining a constant distance between them.

2. **Constant amplitude:** The amplitude of regular waves remains constant as they propagate. This means that the height of the wave crests and depth of the wave troughs do not change over time.

3. **Linear polarization:** Regular waves are linearly polarized, meaning that the electric field vector of the wave oscillates in a single plane. This is in contrast to circularly or elliptically polarized waves, which have electric field vectors that rotate in a circular or elliptical path.

4. **Constant wavelength:** The wavelength of regular waves remains constant as they propagate. This means that the distance between successive wave crests or troughs does not change over time.

5. **Constant frequency:** The frequency of regular waves remains constant as they propagate. This means that the number of wave cycles that pass a given point in a given time does not change.

These characteristics make regular waves ideal for studying the basic principles of wave propagation and interaction. They also make them a fundamental concept in the study of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems.

#### 3.1b Regular Wave Equations

The propagation of regular waves can be described by the wave equation, which is a second-order linear partial differential equation. The wave equation describes how the wave's amplitude, frequency, and wavelength are related to the medium's properties and the wave's speed.

The wave equation for regular waves in a homogeneous medium is given by:

$$
\frac{\partial^2 \phi}{\partial t^2} = c^2 \frac{\partial^2 \phi}{\partial x^2}
$$

where $\phi$ is the wave function, $t$ is time, $x$ is the spatial coordinate, and $c$ is the wave speed.

The wave equation can also be expressed in terms of the wave's amplitude, frequency, and wavelength. This is given by:

$$
A = \frac{1}{2} H e^{i(kx-\omega t)}
$$

where $A$ is the wave amplitude, $H$ is the wave height, $k$ is the wave number, and $\omega$ is the angular frequency.

The wave number and angular frequency are related to the wave's wavelength and frequency by the equations:

$$
k = \frac{2\pi}{\lambda}
$$

and

$$
\omega = 2\pi f
$$

where $\lambda$ is the wavelength and $f$ is the frequency.

These equations describe the propagation of regular waves in a homogeneous medium. However, in the ocean, the medium is not always homogeneous, and the wave's propagation can be affected by factors such as water depth, ocean currents, and the presence of underwater structures. Therefore, more complex equations and models are often used to describe the propagation of ocean waves.

In the next section, we will explore some of these more complex models and their applications in the study of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems.




#### 3.1c Applications of Regular Waves

Regular waves have a wide range of applications in various fields, including oceanography, civil engineering, and telecommunications. In this section, we will discuss some of the key applications of regular waves.

##### Oceanography

In oceanography, regular waves are used to study wave propagation in the ocean. The uniform motion and constant amplitude of regular waves make them ideal for studying the basic principles of wave propagation and interaction. Regular waves are also used in the design and testing of oceanographic instruments, such as wave recorders and wave radar.

##### Civil Engineering

In civil engineering, regular waves are used in the design and testing of structures that are subjected to wave loading, such as bridges, harbors, and offshore platforms. The constant wavelength and frequency of regular waves make them ideal for studying the response of these structures to wave loading. Regular waves are also used in the design of wave attenuators, which are used to reduce wave energy in harbors and marinas.

##### Telecommunications

In telecommunications, regular waves are used in the design and testing of antennas and communication systems. The linear polarization of regular waves makes them ideal for studying the polarization properties of antennas and communication systems. Regular waves are also used in the design of waveguides, which are used to guide electromagnetic waves along a path.

In conclusion, regular waves have a wide range of applications in various fields. Their uniform motion, constant amplitude, linear polarization, and constant wavelength and frequency make them ideal for studying the basic principles of wave propagation and interaction. They also play a crucial role in the design and testing of various structures and systems.




#### 3.2a Definition of Wave Height and Period

Wave height and period are fundamental parameters that describe the characteristics of a wave. They are defined as follows:

##### Wave Height

The wave height, denoted as $H$, is the vertical distance between the crest (highest point) and the trough (lowest point) of a wave. It is a measure of the amplitude of the wave. The wave height is a crucial parameter in the study of wave propagation and interaction with structures. It is particularly important in the design and testing of structures that are subjected to wave loading, such as bridges, harbors, and offshore platforms.

##### Wave Period

The wave period, denoted as $T$, is the time it takes for a wave to complete one full oscillation. It is the inverse of the wave frequency. The wave period is a measure of the time scale of the wave. It is particularly important in the study of wave propagation and interaction with structures. The wave period is also a key parameter in the design and testing of structures that are subjected to wave loading.

In the context of plane progressive regular waves, the wave height and period are constant throughout the wave. This is because plane progressive regular waves have a uniform motion and constant amplitude. The wave height and period are therefore independent of position and time, making them ideal for studying the basic principles of wave propagation and interaction.

The wave height and period are also related to the wavelength and frequency of the wave. The wavelength, denoted as $\lambda$, is the horizontal distance between successive crests or troughs of a wave. It is related to the wave period and frequency by the equation:

$$
\lambda = cT = \frac{1}{f}
$$

where $c$ is the wave speed and $f$ is the wave frequency.

In the next sections, we will discuss the methods for measuring wave height and period, and the factors that affect these parameters.

#### 3.2b Wave Height and Period Measurement Techniques

The measurement of wave height and period is a critical aspect of oceanography and civil engineering. It allows us to understand the characteristics of waves and their interaction with structures. There are several techniques available for measuring wave height and period, each with its own advantages and limitations.

##### Direct Measurement

Direct measurement involves physically measuring the wave height and period. This can be done using a variety of instruments, such as wave recorders, wave radar, and wave gauges. These instruments are designed to accurately measure the wave height and period at a specific location.

##### Indirect Measurement

Indirect measurement involves estimating the wave height and period from other measurements. This can be done using various methods, such as spectral analysis, wave-height estimation algorithms, and wave-height prediction models. These methods are particularly useful when direct measurement is not feasible or practical.

##### Spectral Analysis

Spectral analysis is a method of estimating the wave height and period from a time series of wave data. It involves decomposing the wave data into its constituent frequencies and amplitudes. The wave height and period can then be estimated from the dominant frequencies and amplitudes.

##### Wave-Height Estimation Algorithms

Wave-height estimation algorithms are computer algorithms that estimate the wave height and period from a time series of wave data. These algorithms are based on various mathematical models and assumptions, and they can provide accurate estimates of the wave height and period.

##### Wave-Height Prediction Models

Wave-height prediction models are mathematical models that predict the wave height and period based on various environmental parameters, such as wind speed, air pressure, and sea surface temperature. These models are particularly useful for predicting the wave height and period in the absence of direct measurements.

In the next section, we will discuss the factors that affect the measurement of wave height and period.

#### 3.2c Wave Height and Period Prediction Models

Wave height and period prediction models are mathematical models that predict the wave height and period based on various environmental parameters. These models are particularly useful for predicting the wave height and period in the absence of direct measurements. They are also used to validate wave height and period measurements.

##### Wave Height Prediction Models

Wave height prediction models are mathematical models that predict the wave height based on various environmental parameters. These models are based on the principles of wave propagation and interaction with structures. They take into account the wave frequency, wavelength, and wind speed, among other parameters.

One of the most commonly used wave height prediction models is the significant wave height model. This model predicts the significant wave height, which is the mean wave height of the highest third of the waves. It is defined as four times the standard deviation of the surface elevation, or equivalently as four times the square root of the zeroth-order moment of the wave spectrum.

##### Wave Period Prediction Models

Wave period prediction models are mathematical models that predict the wave period based on various environmental parameters. These models are also based on the principles of wave propagation and interaction with structures. They take into account the wave frequency, wavelength, and wind speed, among other parameters.

One of the most commonly used wave period prediction models is the wave period model. This model predicts the wave period based on the wave frequency and wavelength. It is defined as the inverse of the wave frequency.

##### Wave Height and Period Prediction Models

Wave height and period prediction models are mathematical models that predict both the wave height and period based on various environmental parameters. These models are particularly useful for predicting the wave height and period in the absence of direct measurements. They take into account the wave frequency, wavelength, and wind speed, among other parameters.

One of the most commonly used wave height and period prediction models is the wave spectrum model. This model predicts the wave height and period based on the wave spectrum, which is a representation of the wave energy as a function of frequency and direction. It is defined as the product of the wave height and the wave period.

In the next section, we will discuss the factors that affect the prediction of wave height and period.




#### 3.2b Wave Height and Period Measurement Techniques

The measurement of wave height and period is a crucial aspect of oceanography and marine engineering. It allows us to understand the characteristics of ocean waves, their propagation, and their interaction with structures. There are several techniques for measuring wave height and period, each with its own advantages and limitations.

##### Direct Measurement Techniques

Direct measurement techniques involve physically measuring the wave height and period. This can be done using a variety of instruments, including wave gauges, wave riders, and wave radar.

###### Wave Gauges

Wave gauges are instruments that measure the wave height by directly immersing a sensor in the water. They can be either mechanical or electronic. Mechanical wave gauges, such as the oscillating water column (OWC) wave gauge, use a pendulum to measure the wave height. Electronic wave gauges, on the other hand, use sensors such as accelerometers or pressure sensors to measure the wave height.

###### Wave Riders

Wave riders are instruments that ride on top of the wave and measure the wave height and period as they move along with the wave. They can be either mechanical or electronic. Mechanical wave riders, such as the oscillating water column (OWC) wave rider, use a pendulum to measure the wave height. Electronic wave riders, on the other hand, use sensors such as accelerometers or pressure sensors to measure the wave height.

###### Wave Radar

Wave radar is a relatively new technique for measuring wave height and period. It uses radar technology to measure the wave height and period from a distance. This technique is particularly useful for measuring the wave height and period of large waves, such as tsunamis.

##### Indirect Measurement Techniques

Indirect measurement techniques involve inferring the wave height and period from other measurements. This can be done using various methods, including spectral analysis, wave model simulations, and remote sensing.

###### Spectral Analysis

Spectral analysis is a method for determining the wave height and period from a time series of wave measurements. It involves decomposing the time series into its constituent frequencies and amplitudes. The wave height and period can then be determined from the dominant frequencies and amplitudes.

###### Wave Model Simulations

Wave model simulations involve using numerical models to simulate the propagation of waves. These models can be used to infer the wave height and period from the simulated wave fields.

###### Remote Sensing

Remote sensing is a method for measuring the wave height and period from a distance. It involves using satellite or airborne sensors to measure the wave height and period. This technique is particularly useful for measuring the wave height and period of large waves, such as tsunamis.

In the next section, we will discuss the factors that affect the measurement of wave height and period.




#### 3.2c Effects of Wave Height and Period on Wave Dynamics

The wave height and period are two fundamental parameters that describe the characteristics of an ocean wave. They are closely related to the wave's energy, propagation speed, and the forces it exerts on structures. In this section, we will explore the effects of wave height and period on wave dynamics.

##### Wave Energy and Propagation Speed

The wave height and period are directly related to the wave's energy and propagation speed. The wave energy is proportional to the square of the wave height, as described by the equation:

$$
E = \frac{1}{2} \rho g A^2
$$

where $E$ is the wave energy, $\rho$ is the density of the water, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, and $A$ is the wave height. The wave propagation speed, on the other hand, is inversely proportional to the wave period, as described by the equation:

$$
c = \frac{gT}{2\pi}
$$

where $c$ is the wave propagation speed, $T$ is the wave period, and $g$ and $\pi$ are constants. These equations show that larger wave heights and shorter periods result in higher wave energies and faster propagation speeds.

##### Wave Forces on Structures

The wave height and period also play a crucial role in the forces exerted by waves on structures. The wave force is proportional to the square of the wave height and the wave period, as described by the equation:

$$
F = \frac{1}{2} \rho g A^2 T^2
$$

where $F$ is the wave force. This equation shows that larger wave heights and shorter periods result in higher wave forces. This is particularly important in the design of offshore energy systems and ships, which must be able to withstand these forces.

##### Wave Breaking and Dissipation

The wave height and period also affect the breaking and dissipation of waves. Larger wave heights and shorter periods can lead to more intense wave breaking, which can result in the generation of turbulence and mixing in the water. This can have significant implications for the transport of heat, salt, and other properties in the ocean.

In conclusion, the wave height and period are fundamental parameters that have a profound impact on wave dynamics. Understanding their effects is crucial for a wide range of applications, from the design of offshore energy systems to the prediction of wave propagation and breaking.




#### 3.3a Definition of Wave Amplitude and Phase

The amplitude and phase of a wave are fundamental properties that describe the wave's shape and position in its cycle. 

##### Wave Amplitude

The amplitude of a wave is the maximum displacement of the wave from its mean or equilibrium position. It is a measure of the wave's strength or energy. The amplitude of a wave is directly related to the wave's energy, as described by the equation:

$$
E = \frac{1}{2} \rho g A^2
$$

where $E$ is the wave energy, $\rho$ is the density of the water, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, and $A$ is the wave amplitude. Larger amplitudes result in higher wave energies and faster propagation speeds.

##### Wave Phase

The phase of a wave is the position of the wave in its cycle. It is measured in radians and can range from 0 to $2\pi$. The phase of a wave is used to describe the relative position of the wave with respect to a reference point. The phase of a wave is defined as the angle from the 12:00 position to the current position of the hand, at time $t$, measured clockwise.

The phase of a wave is particularly useful when the origin $t_0$ is chosen based on features of the wave. For example, for a sinusoid, a convenient choice is any $t$ where the function's value changes from zero to positive.

The phase expressed in degrees (from 0 to 360, or from 180 to +180) is defined the same way, except with "360" in place of "2".

##### Consequences

The phase of a wave is periodic, with the same period $T$ as the wave. This means that the phase of a wave repeats itself after every period. The phase of a wave is zero at the start of each period, that is:

$$
\phi(t) = 0 \quad \text{for } t = 0, T, 2T, \ldots
$$

Moreover, for any given choice of the origin $t_0$, the value of the wave $F(t)$ for any argument $t$ depends only on its phase at $t$. Namely, one can write $F(t) = A \sin(\phi(t))$, where $A$ is the amplitude of the wave and $\phi(t)$ is the phase of the wave at time $t$.

#### 3.3b Wave Amplitude and Phase in Regular Waves

In the previous section, we defined the amplitude and phase of a wave. Now, let's delve into the specifics of these properties in regular waves. 

##### Wave Amplitude in Regular Waves

In regular waves, the amplitude remains constant throughout the wave cycle. This is because regular waves are sinusoidal, and the amplitude of a sinusoidal wave is constant. The amplitude of a regular wave is a measure of the wave's strength or energy. Larger amplitudes result in higher wave energies and faster propagation speeds.

The amplitude of a regular wave can be calculated using the equation:

$$
A = \sqrt{E / \rho g}
$$

where $A$ is the amplitude, $E$ is the wave energy, $\rho$ is the density of the water, and $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity.

##### Wave Phase in Regular Waves

In regular waves, the phase is also constant throughout the wave cycle. This is because regular waves are sinusoidal, and the phase of a sinusoidal wave is constant. The phase of a regular wave is used to describe the relative position of the wave with respect to a reference point.

The phase of a regular wave can be calculated using the equation:

$$
\phi(t) = \omega t + \phi_0
$$

where $\phi(t)$ is the phase, $\omega$ is the angular frequency, $t$ is the time, and $\phi_0$ is the initial phase.

The initial phase $\phi_0$ is chosen based on features of the wave. For example, for a sinusoid, a convenient choice is any $t$ where the function's value changes from zero to positive.

The phase expressed in degrees (from 0 to 360, or from 180 to +180) is defined the same way, except with "360" in place of "2".

##### Consequences

The phase of a regular wave is periodic, with the same period $T$ as the wave. This means that the phase of a regular wave repeats itself after every period. The phase of a regular wave is zero at the start of each period, that is:

$$
\phi(t) = 0 \quad \text{for } t = 0, T, 2T, \ldots
$$

Moreover, for any given choice of the origin $t_0$, the value of the wave $F(t)$ for any argument $t$ depends only on its phase at $t$. Namely, one can write $F(t) = A \sin(\phi(t))$, where $A$ is the amplitude of the wave and $\phi(t)$ is the phase of the wave at time $t$.

#### 3.3c Wave Amplitude and Phase in Irregular Waves

In the previous sections, we have discussed the amplitude and phase of regular waves. Now, let's explore these properties in irregular waves. 

##### Wave Amplitude in Irregular Waves

In irregular waves, the amplitude can vary throughout the wave cycle. This is because irregular waves are not sinusoidal, and the amplitude of a wave is a measure of the wave's strength or energy. Larger amplitudes result in higher wave energies and faster propagation speeds.

The amplitude of an irregular wave can be calculated using the equation:

$$
A = \sqrt{E / \rho g}
$$

where $A$ is the amplitude, $E$ is the wave energy, $\rho$ is the density of the water, and $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity.

##### Wave Phase in Irregular Waves

In irregular waves, the phase can also vary throughout the wave cycle. This is because irregular waves are not sinusoidal, and the phase of a wave is used to describe the relative position of the wave with respect to a reference point.

The phase of an irregular wave can be calculated using the equation:

$$
\phi(t) = \omega t + \phi_0
$$

where $\phi(t)$ is the phase, $\omega$ is the angular frequency, $t$ is the time, and $\phi_0$ is the initial phase.

The initial phase $\phi_0$ is chosen based on features of the wave. For example, for a sinusoid, a convenient choice is any $t$ where the function's value changes from zero to positive.

The phase expressed in degrees (from 0 to 360, or from 180 to +180) is defined the same way, except with "360" in place of "2".

##### Consequences

The phase of an irregular wave is not periodic, unlike the phase of a regular wave. This is because the phase of an irregular wave can change significantly over a single period. The phase of an irregular wave is zero at the start of each period, that is:

$$
\phi(t) = 0 \quad \text{for } t = 0, T, 2T, \ldots
$$

Moreover, for any given choice of the origin $t_0$, the value of the wave $F(t)$ for any argument $t$ depends only on its phase at $t$. Namely, one can write $F(t) = A \sin(\phi(t))$, where $A$ is the amplitude of the wave and $\phi(t)$ is the phase of the wave at time $t$.




#### 3.3b Measurement of Wave Amplitude and Phase

The measurement of wave amplitude and phase is a crucial aspect of understanding and predicting the behavior of ocean waves. This section will discuss the various methods used to measure wave amplitude and phase, including the use of wave gauges and the least-squares spectral analysis (LSSA) method.

##### Wave Gauges

Wave gauges are instruments used to measure the amplitude and phase of ocean waves. They work by measuring the displacement of the water surface due to the passing wave. The most common type of wave gauge is the pressure gauge, which measures the pressure difference between the water surface and a fixed point. This pressure difference is then converted into a displacement, which is proportional to the wave amplitude.

The phase of the wave can be measured by observing the time at which the wave reaches a certain point. This time is then used to calculate the phase of the wave relative to a reference point.

##### Least-Squares Spectral Analysis (LSSA)

The LSSA method is a mathematical technique used to estimate the amplitude and phase of a wave signal. It involves computing the least-squares spectrum by performing the least-squares approximation multiple times, each time for a different frequency.

The LSSA method treats each sinusoidal component independently, even though they may not be orthogonal to data points. This is in contrast to the simultaneous or in-context method, which can fit more components (sines and cosines) than there are data samples.

The LSSA method involves the following steps:

1. Compute the least-squares spectrum by performing the least-squares approximation multiple times, each time for a different frequency.
2. For each frequency, sine and cosine functions are evaluated at the times corresponding to the data samples.
3. Dot products of the data vector with the sinusoid vectors are taken and appropriately normalized.
4. A time shift is calculated for each frequency to orthogonalize the sine and cosine components before the dot product.
5. A power is computed from those two amplitude components.

This process implements a discrete Fourier transform when the data are uniformly spaced in time and the frequencies chosen correspond to integer numbers of cycles over the finite data record.

In the next section, we will discuss the application of these methods in the study of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems.

#### 3.3c Wave Amplitude and Phase in Nonlinear Systems

In the previous sections, we have discussed the measurement of wave amplitude and phase in linear systems. However, many real-world systems, including ocean waves and offshore energy systems, exhibit nonlinear behavior. In this section, we will explore the concept of wave amplitude and phase in nonlinear systems.

Nonlinear systems are those in which the output is not directly proportional to the input. In the context of ocean waves, this nonlinearity can arise due to various factors such as wave-wave interactions, wave-current interactions, and wave-structure interactions. These nonlinearities can significantly affect the amplitude and phase of the waves, leading to complex wave patterns that are difficult to predict using linear theories.

One of the key tools for studying nonlinear systems is the method of multiple scales. This method involves introducing a small parameter $\epsilon$ that characterizes the nonlinearity of the system. The solution to the system is then sought in the form of a power series expansion in $\epsilon$, with each term representing a different order of nonlinearity.

For example, consider a nonlinear wave equation of the form:

$$
\frac{\partial^2 \eta}{\partial t^2} = c^2 \frac{\partial^2 \eta}{\partial x^2} + \epsilon g \eta \frac{\partial \eta}{\partial x}
$$

where $\eta$ is the wave amplitude, $t$ is time, $x$ is the spatial coordinate, $c$ is the wave speed, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, and $\epsilon$ is the small parameter characterizing the nonlinearity of the system.

The solution to this equation can be sought in the form:

$$
\eta(x,t) = \eta_0(x,t) + \epsilon \eta_1(x,t) + \epsilon^2 \eta_2(x,t) + \cdots
$$

where $\eta_0(x,t)$ is the linear solution, $\eta_1(x,t)$ is the first-order nonlinear correction, and so on.

By substituting this series into the wave equation and equating terms of the same order in $\epsilon$, we can obtain a set of equations that can be solved to obtain the wave amplitude and phase at each order of nonlinearity.

In the next section, we will discuss some specific examples of nonlinear wave phenomena and how they can be studied using the method of multiple scales.




#### 3.3c Effects of Wave Amplitude and Phase on Wave Dynamics

The amplitude and phase of ocean waves play a crucial role in their dynamics. The amplitude of a wave is directly related to its energy, with larger amplitudes indicating higher energy levels. The phase of a wave, on the other hand, determines its position in the wave cycle.

##### Wave Amplitude and Energy

The amplitude of a wave is a measure of the displacement of the water surface from its mean position. This displacement is directly related to the energy of the wave. The larger the amplitude, the more energy the wave carries. This energy can be transferred to other waves or to structures in the ocean, such as ships or offshore energy systems.

The energy of a wave can be calculated using the formula:

$$
E = \frac{1}{2} \rho g A^2
$$

where $E$ is the energy, $\rho$ is the density of the water, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, and $A$ is the amplitude of the wave.

##### Wave Phase and Wave Propagation

The phase of a wave determines its position in the wave cycle. It is the angle between the wave crest and a reference point, usually the mean water level. The phase of a wave can affect its interaction with other waves and structures.

For example, when two waves with different phases meet, they can either enhance or cancel each other out, depending on their phase difference. This phenomenon is known as wave interference.

The phase of a wave can also affect its propagation. The phase velocity of a wave, which is the speed at which the phase of the wave propagates, can be different from the group velocity, which is the speed at which the wave energy propagates. This difference can lead to phenomena such as wave steepening and wave breaking.

##### Wave Amplitude and Phase in Nonlinear Systems

In nonlinear systems, the amplitude and phase of waves can interact in complex ways. For example, in the three-wave interaction described by the three-wave equation, the amplitude and phase of the three waves can interact to produce a variety of solutions, including explosive solutions, stimulated backscatter, and soliton exchange.

These solutions can have significant implications for the dynamics of ocean waves and their interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. For example, the simulton solution, which consists of three comoving solitons, can be related to the "three sisters" observed in rogue waves, even though deep water does not have a three-wave resonant interaction.

In conclusion, the amplitude and phase of ocean waves are fundamental to their dynamics. Understanding these properties is crucial for predicting and managing the interaction of waves with ships and offshore energy systems.




#### 3.4a Definition of Wave Velocity

Wave velocity is a fundamental concept in the study of ocean waves. It refers to the speed at which a wave propagates through a medium. The velocity of a wave can be described in terms of its phase velocity and group velocity.

##### Phase Velocity

The phase velocity of a wave, denoted as $v_p$, is the speed at which the phase of the wave propagates. It is defined as the ratio of the angular frequency $\omega$ to the wavenumber $k$:

$$
v_p = \frac{\omega}{k}
$$

The phase velocity is a measure of how quickly the phase of the wave changes as it propagates. It is a constant for a given wave and is independent of the frequency of the wave.

##### Group Velocity

The group velocity of a wave, denoted as $v_g$, is the speed at which the wave packet (or group) of waves propagates. It is defined as the derivative of the dispersion relation with respect to the wavenumber:

$$
v_g = \frac{\partial \omega}{\partial k}
$$

The group velocity is a measure of how quickly the energy of the wave packet propagates. It can vary with frequency and is typically larger than the phase velocity.

##### Relationship between Phase Velocity and Group Velocity

The phase velocity and group velocity are related by the dispersion relation of the medium. For a non-dispersive medium, where the dispersion relation is independent of the frequency, the phase velocity and group velocity are equal. However, for a dispersive medium, where the dispersion relation depends on the frequency, the phase velocity and group velocity can be different.

In the next section, we will discuss the factors that affect wave velocity and how it influences the interaction of waves with ships and offshore energy systems.

#### 3.4b Factors Affecting Wave Velocity

Several factors can affect the velocity of ocean waves. These factors can be broadly categorized into two groups: external factors and internal factors.

##### External Factors

External factors are those that are not inherent to the wave but can influence its velocity. These include:

1. **Wind Speed and Direction**: Wind is a primary source of ocean waves. The speed and direction of the wind can significantly affect the velocity of the waves. Stronger winds can generate larger waves with higher velocities. The direction of the wind can also determine the direction of wave propagation.

2. **Water Depth**: The depth of the water can affect the velocity of the waves. In shallow water, the velocity of the waves can be lower due to the increased friction with the seafloor. In deep water, the velocity can be higher due to the reduced friction.

3. **Tidal Forces**: Tidal forces can also influence the velocity of the waves, particularly in coastal areas. The gravitational pull of the moon and the sun can cause the ocean water to bulge out towards and away from these celestial bodies, leading to tidal forces. These forces can cause the waves to move at different velocities in different directions.

##### Internal Factors

Internal factors are inherent to the wave and can influence its velocity. These include:

1. **Wave Frequency**: The frequency of the wave can affect its velocity. Higher frequency waves tend to have higher velocities. This is due to the dispersion relation, which relates the frequency and wavenumber of the wave.

2. **Wave Amplitude**: The amplitude of the wave can also affect its velocity. Larger amplitude waves can have higher velocities due to the increased energy content.

3. **Wave Steepness**: The steepness of the wave, defined as the ratio of the wave height to the wavelength, can influence the velocity of the wave. Steeper waves can have higher velocities due to the increased wave energy.

In the next section, we will discuss how these factors can influence the interaction of waves with ships and offshore energy systems.

#### 3.4c Wave Velocity in Different Media

The velocity of ocean waves can vary significantly depending on the medium through which they propagate. The medium can be broadly categorized into two types: homogeneous and inhomogeneous.

##### Homogeneous Media

Homogeneous media are those in which the properties (such as density and elasticity) are uniform throughout. In such media, the wave velocity is typically constant and independent of the position. The wave velocity in homogeneous media can be calculated using the dispersion relation:

$$
v = \sqrt{\frac{g \lambda}{2\pi}}
$$

where $v$ is the wave velocity, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, $\lambda$ is the wavelength, and $\pi$ is the pi constant.

##### Inhomogeneous Media

In contrast, inhomogeneous media are those in which the properties vary with position. The wave velocity in such media can vary significantly and can depend on the position. The wave velocity in inhomogeneous media can be calculated using the dispersion relation:

$$
v = \sqrt{\frac{g \lambda}{2\pi} \frac{1}{k}}
$$

where $k$ is the wavenumber. The wavenumber can be calculated using the dispersion relation:

$$
k = \frac{2\pi}{\lambda}
$$

In inhomogeneous media, the wave velocity can be affected by various factors, including changes in water depth, changes in water density, and changes in the seafloor topography.

##### Effects of Inhomogeneity on Wave Velocity

Inhomogeneity can have significant effects on the velocity of ocean waves. For example, in the case of a wave propagating over a seafloor with a varying depth, the wave velocity can change as the wave moves from deep water to shallow water. This can lead to changes in the wave energy and the wave amplitude.

Inhomogeneity can also lead to wave refraction, where the wave changes its direction as it propagates. This can be particularly significant in coastal areas, where the wave can be affected by changes in the water depth and the seafloor topography.

In the next section, we will discuss how these factors can influence the interaction of waves with ships and offshore energy systems.




#### 3.4b Factors Affecting Wave Velocity

Several factors can affect the velocity of ocean waves. These factors can be broadly categorized into two groups: external factors and internal factors.

##### External Factors

External factors are those that are not inherent to the wave but can influence its velocity. These include:

1. **Atmospheric Conditions**: The state of the atmosphere can significantly affect the velocity of ocean waves. Wind speed and direction, air pressure, and temperature can all influence the generation and propagation of waves. For instance, strong winds can generate large waves with high velocities, while low air pressure can decrease the velocity of waves.

2. **Tidal Forces**: The gravitational pull of the moon and the sun can cause tidal forces that can affect the velocity of ocean waves. These forces can cause changes in the water level, which can in turn affect the velocity of waves.

3. **Geographical Features**: The shape and depth of the ocean floor can also influence the velocity of waves. Shallow waters and narrow channels can cause waves to slow down, while deep waters and wide channels can increase wave velocity.

##### Internal Factors

Internal factors are inherent to the wave and can influence its velocity. These include:

1. **Wave Frequency**: The frequency of a wave can affect its velocity. Higher frequency waves tend to have higher velocities.

2. **Wave Amplitude**: The amplitude of a wave can also influence its velocity. Larger amplitude waves tend to have higher velocities.

3. **Wave Direction**: The direction of wave propagation can affect its velocity. Waves propagating in the direction of the wind can have higher velocities than waves propagating against the wind.

Understanding these factors is crucial for predicting the velocity of ocean waves and for designing ships and offshore energy systems that can withstand these waves. In the next section, we will discuss how these factors can be measured and modeled.

#### 3.4c Wave Velocity Measurement Techniques

The measurement of wave velocity is a critical aspect of oceanography and marine engineering. It allows us to understand the behavior of waves in different environments and to predict their impact on ships and offshore energy systems. There are several techniques available for measuring wave velocity, each with its own advantages and limitations.

##### Direct Measurement Techniques

Direct measurement techniques involve directly measuring the velocity of waves. These techniques include:

1. **Doppler Radar**: Doppler radar can be used to measure the velocity of waves by detecting the Doppler shift in the radar signal caused by the motion of the waves. This technique can provide high-resolution measurements of wave velocity, but it requires a clear line of sight to the waves and can be affected by atmospheric conditions.

2. **Acoustic Doppler Current Profilers (ADCPs)**: ADCPs use sound waves to measure the velocity of water, including waves. They can provide three-dimensional measurements of wave velocity, but they can be expensive and require specialized equipment and training.

##### Indirect Measurement Techniques

Indirect measurement techniques involve inferring the velocity of waves from other measurements. These techniques include:

1. **Wave Spectrum Analysis**: Wave spectrum analysis involves measuring the amplitude and frequency of waves and using this information to calculate the wave velocity. This technique can provide accurate measurements of wave velocity, but it requires a large number of measurements and can be affected by noise.

2. **Tidal Gauges**: Tidal gauges can be used to measure the velocity of waves by detecting changes in the water level caused by the waves. This technique can provide long-term measurements of wave velocity, but it can be affected by sedimentation and biofouling.

##### Numerical Modeling Techniques

Numerical modeling techniques involve using mathematical models to simulate the propagation of waves. These techniques include:

1. **Numerical Weather Prediction (NWP) Models**: NWP models use numerical methods to solve the primitive equations of fluid dynamics to simulate the propagation of waves. These models can provide detailed information about wave velocity, but they require high-resolution data and can be computationally intensive.

2. **Wave Modeling Software**: Wave modeling software, such as TOMAWAC and MIKE 21, use simplified equations to simulate the propagation of waves. These models can provide quick and easy measurements of wave velocity, but they may not be as accurate as NWP models.

In conclusion, the choice of wave velocity measurement technique depends on the specific requirements of the application, including the accuracy and resolution of the measurements, the available resources, and the environmental conditions.




#### 3.4c Effects of Wave Velocity on Wave Dynamics

The velocity of ocean waves plays a crucial role in their dynamics. It influences the wave's propagation, energy transfer, and interaction with other waves and structures. In this section, we will explore the effects of wave velocity on wave dynamics, focusing on the effects of wave velocity on wave propagation and energy transfer.

##### Effects of Wave Velocity on Wave Propagation

The velocity of a wave is directly related to its propagation speed. The propagation speed of a wave is the speed at which the wave travels through the medium. For ocean waves, the medium is the water. The propagation speed of a wave is determined by its frequency and the properties of the medium.

The propagation speed of a wave can be calculated using the dispersion relation, which relates the wave's frequency to its wavelength and propagation speed. For ocean waves, the dispersion relation can be written as:

$$
\omega = \sqrt{gk}
$$

where $\omega$ is the frequency, $k$ is the wavenumber, and $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity. This equation shows that the propagation speed of a wave is proportional to the square root of the product of the frequency and the wavenumber.

The propagation speed of a wave can also be affected by the wave's amplitude and the properties of the medium. For instance, in deep water, the propagation speed of a wave is primarily determined by its frequency and the acceleration due to gravity. However, in shallow water, the propagation speed can be affected by the wave's amplitude and the water depth.

##### Effects of Wave Velocity on Energy Transfer

The velocity of a wave also affects its energy transfer. The energy transfer of a wave is the rate at which energy is transferred from one point to another. For ocean waves, the energy transfer can be calculated using the Poynting vector, which is a vector that represents the energy flux of a wave.

The Poynting vector can be written as:

$$
\mathbf{P} = \frac{1}{\mu_0} \mathbf{E} \times \mathbf{H}
$$

where $\mathbf{E}$ is the electric field, $\mathbf{H}$ is the magnetic field, and $\mu_0$ is the permeability of free space. This equation shows that the energy transfer of a wave is proportional to the product of the electric and magnetic fields.

The energy transfer of a wave can be affected by its velocity. For instance, the energy transfer of a wave can increase with its velocity, leading to higher energy transfer rates. However, the energy transfer can also decrease with the velocity, leading to lower energy transfer rates.

In conclusion, the velocity of ocean waves plays a crucial role in their dynamics. It affects the wave's propagation and energy transfer, and can be influenced by various factors such as the wave's frequency, amplitude, and the properties of the medium. Understanding the effects of wave velocity on wave dynamics is essential for predicting and managing the impact of ocean waves on ships and offshore energy systems.

## Chapter: Nonlinear Waves

### Introduction

The study of ocean waves is a complex and fascinating field, and one of the most intriguing aspects of this discipline is the exploration of nonlinear waves. This chapter, "Nonlinear Waves," delves into the intricate world of nonlinear wave phenomena, providing a comprehensive guide to understanding and interpreting these waves.

Nonlinear waves are a type of wave that do not follow the principle of superposition, meaning the sum of two or more nonlinear waves is not equal to the sum of their individual components. This nonlinearity can lead to a variety of interesting and complex behaviors, including wave steepening, shock formation, and soliton generation. These phenomena are not only of theoretical interest, but also have practical implications for a wide range of fields, from oceanography to engineering.

In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental principles of nonlinear wave theory, including the mathematical models that describe these waves. We will also discuss the various types of nonlinear waves, such as solitons and shock waves, and their unique properties. Furthermore, we will examine the interaction of nonlinear waves with ships and offshore energy systems, providing insights into the challenges and opportunities presented by these complex wave phenomena.

The study of nonlinear waves is a rapidly evolving field, with new discoveries and advancements being made on a regular basis. This chapter aims to provide a solid foundation in the principles and theories of nonlinear waves, while also highlighting some of the most recent developments in this exciting area of research.

Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a professional in the field of oceanography or engineering, we hope that this chapter will serve as a valuable resource in your exploration of nonlinear waves. We invite you to join us on this journey into the fascinating world of nonlinear waves, where the simple and the complex intertwine to create a rich tapestry of wave phenomena.

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will delve into the concept of wave energy density and flux, two fundamental concepts in the study of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. Wave energy density and flux are crucial in understanding the behavior of waves and their impact on various systems. They provide a quantitative measure of the energy contained in a wave and the rate at which this energy is transported.

Wave energy density, often denoted as $E$, is defined as the energy per unit area of the wave. It is a measure of the intensity of the wave and is typically expressed in units of J/m$^2$. The energy density of a wave is directly related to its amplitude and frequency, with higher amplitude and frequency waves having higher energy densities.

On the other hand, wave energy flux, often denoted as $F$, is defined as the rate of energy transport per unit area. It is a measure of the power of the wave and is typically expressed in units of W/m$^2$. The energy flux of a wave is directly related to its energy density and propagation direction, with waves propagating in the direction of increasing energy flux.

Together, wave energy density and flux provide a comprehensive understanding of the energy content and transport of waves. They are essential in the design and analysis of ships and offshore energy systems, as they help determine the forces and loads exerted by waves on these systems.

In the following sections, we will explore the mathematical representations of wave energy density and flux, their physical interpretations, and their applications in the field of ocean wave interaction. We will also discuss the factors that influence wave energy density and flux, such as wave height, wavelength, and water depth. By the end of this chapter, readers should have a solid understanding of these concepts and their importance in the study of ocean wave interaction.




#### 4.1a Energy Conservation Equations for Waves

In the previous section, we introduced the concepts of wave energy density and flux. In this section, we will explore the energy conservation equations for waves, which are fundamental to understanding the behavior of waves in the ocean.

The energy conservation equation for waves is derived from the general equation of heat transfer, which can be written as:

$$
\rho {\partial k\over{\partial t}} = -\rho {\bf v}\cdot\nabla k - \rho {\bf v}\cdot\nabla h + \rho T{\bf v}\cdot \nabla s + \nabla\cdot(\sigma\cdot {\bf v}) - \sigma_{ij}{\partial v_{i}\over{\partial x_{j}}}
$$

where $\rho$ is the density, $k$ is the kinetic energy per unit mass, $v$ is the velocity, $h$ is the enthalpy, $T$ is the temperature, $s$ is the entropy, and $\sigma$ is the stress tensor.

The equation for entropy production can be written as:

$$
\rho T {Ds\over{Dt}} = \nabla\cdot(\kappa\nabla T) + {\mu\over{2}}\left( {\partial v_{i}\over{\partial x_{j}}} + {\partial v_{j}\over{\partial x_{i}}} - {2\over{3}}\delta_{ij}\nabla\cdot {\bf v} \right)^{2} + \zeta(\nabla \cdot {\bf v})^{2}
$$

In the case where thermal conduction and viscous forces are absent, the equation for entropy production collapses to $Ds/Dt=0$, showing that ideal fluid flow is isentropic.

These equations are derived in Section 49 of the sixth volume of L.D. Landau and E.M. Lifshitz's "Course of Theoretical Physics". They can be used to measure the heat transfer and air flow in a domestic refrigerator, to do a harmonic analysis of regenerators, or to understand the physics of glaciers.

In the context of ocean wave interaction, these equations are crucial in understanding the energy transfer between waves and ships or offshore energy systems. They provide a quantitative measure of the energy exchange, which is essential in the design and analysis of these systems.

In the next section, we will explore the mathematical representations of wave energy density and flux in more detail, and discuss their physical interpretations and applications.

#### 4.1b Wave Energy Density and Flux Calculation

In the previous section, we introduced the energy conservation equations for waves. These equations are fundamental to understanding the behavior of waves in the ocean. In this section, we will delve deeper into the calculation of wave energy density and flux, which are crucial in the study of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems.

The wave energy density, $E$, is defined as the energy per unit area of the wave. It is a measure of the intensity of the wave and is typically expressed in units of J/m$^2$. The wave energy density can be calculated using the equation:

$$
E = \frac{1}{2}\rho g \eta^2
$$

where $\rho$ is the density of the fluid, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, and $\eta$ is the wave amplitude.

The wave energy flux, $F$, is defined as the rate of energy transport per unit area. It is a measure of the power of the wave and is typically expressed in units of W/m$^2$. The wave energy flux can be calculated using the equation:

$$
F = \frac{1}{2}\rho g \eta^2 \omega
$$

where $\omega$ is the angular frequency of the wave.

These equations are derived from the energy conservation equations for waves, which we introduced in the previous section. They provide a quantitative measure of the energy content and transport of waves, which is essential in the design and analysis of ships and offshore energy systems.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of wave energy spectrum, which is a more comprehensive representation of the energy content of a wave field. We will also discuss the methods for measuring wave energy density and flux in the ocean.

#### 4.1c Wave Energy Density and Flux Applications

In this section, we will explore some of the applications of wave energy density and flux in the study of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. These applications are crucial in understanding the behavior of waves and their impact on various systems.

One of the primary applications of wave energy density and flux is in the design and analysis of ships. The wave energy density and flux can be used to calculate the wave forces acting on a ship, which are crucial in determining the ship's stability and safety. For instance, the wave energy flux can be used to calculate the wave force acting on a ship's hull, which is essential in designing a ship that can withstand the forces exerted by waves.

Another important application of wave energy density and flux is in the design and analysis of offshore energy systems. These systems, such as wind turbines and wave energy converters, are often exposed to harsh wave conditions. The wave energy density and flux can be used to calculate the wave forces acting on these systems, which are crucial in determining the system's structural integrity and performance.

The wave energy density and flux can also be used in the study of wave propagation in the ocean. By analyzing the wave energy density and flux, we can gain insights into the propagation of waves, which is crucial in understanding the behavior of waves in the ocean. This can be particularly useful in predicting the impact of waves on coastal areas and offshore structures.

In addition, the wave energy density and flux can be used in the study of wave-wave interactions. These interactions, which involve the transfer of energy between waves, are crucial in understanding the behavior of wave fields in the ocean. By analyzing the wave energy density and flux, we can gain insights into these interactions, which can be useful in predicting the behavior of wave fields in the ocean.
