#### 4.1b Applications of Energy Conservation in Wave Dynamics

The principles of energy conservation are fundamental to understanding the behavior of waves in the ocean. They are applied in a variety of ways, from the design of ships and offshore energy systems to the study of wave propagation and interaction with these systems.

#### Ship Design

In the design of ships, energy conservation is used to optimize the ship's performance and safety. The ship's hull is designed to minimize wave resistance, which is the energy lost to the surrounding water due to wave generation. This is achieved by optimizing the hull shape to minimize the wave-making drag, which is a direct application of the energy conservation principle.

The energy conservation principle is also used in the design of the ship's propulsion system. The propeller is designed to convert the engine's power into thrust, which propels the ship forward. The efficiency of this conversion process is maximized by optimizing the propeller's design, which is another application of the energy conservation principle.

#### Offshore Energy Systems

In the design of offshore energy systems, such as wind turbines and wave energy converters, energy conservation is used to optimize the system's performance and efficiency. The system's design is optimized to maximize the energy extraction from the waves, which is achieved by optimizing the system's shape and placement in the water.

The energy conservation principle is also used in the design of the system's power conversion system. The power converter is designed to convert the wave energy into electrical power, which is maximized by optimizing the converter's design. This is another application of the energy conservation principle.

#### Wave Propagation and Interaction

In the study of wave propagation and interaction with ships and offshore energy systems, energy conservation is used to understand the wave behavior. The wave energy is conserved as it propagates through the water, which is a direct application of the energy conservation principle.

The energy conservation principle is also used to understand the wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. The wave energy is transferred to the system, which is a direct application of the energy conservation principle. This understanding is crucial in the design and analysis of these systems.

In conclusion, the principles of energy conservation are fundamental to understanding the behavior of waves in the ocean. They are applied in a variety of ways, from the design of ships and offshore energy systems to the study of wave propagation and interaction with these systems.




#### 4.1c Energy Conservation in Wave Energy Systems

In the context of wave energy systems, energy conservation is a fundamental principle that governs the operation of these systems. The principle of energy conservation is applied in the design and operation of wave energy converters, which are devices that convert wave energy into electrical power.

#### Wave Energy Converters

Wave energy converters operate on the principle of energy conservation. The wave energy is converted into electrical power, which is a direct application of the energy conservation principle. The efficiency of this conversion process is maximized by optimizing the converter's design, which is another application of the energy conservation principle.

The design of wave energy converters involves a complex interplay of various physical principles, including fluid dynamics, structural mechanics, and electrical engineering. The converter's design is optimized to maximize the energy extraction from the waves, which is achieved by optimizing the converter's shape and placement in the water.

The operation of wave energy converters involves the conversion of wave energy into electrical power. This conversion process is governed by the principle of energy conservation, which states that the total energy in a closed system remains constant. In the context of wave energy converters, this means that the wave energy is converted into electrical power, with no energy loss.

#### Energy Conservation in Wave Energy Systems

In wave energy systems, energy conservation is a fundamental principle that governs the operation of these systems. The principle of energy conservation is applied in the design and operation of wave energy converters, which are devices that convert wave energy into electrical power.

The design of wave energy converters involves a complex interplay of various physical principles, including fluid dynamics, structural mechanics, and electrical engineering. The converter's design is optimized to maximize the energy extraction from the waves, which is achieved by optimizing the converter's shape and placement in the water.

The operation of wave energy converters involves the conversion of wave energy into electrical power. This conversion process is governed by the principle of energy conservation, which states that the total energy in a closed system remains constant. In the context of wave energy converters, this means that the wave energy is converted into electrical power, with no energy loss.




#### 4.2a Definition of Wave Energy Density

Wave energy density is a fundamental concept in the study of ocean waves and their interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. It is a measure of the energy contained in a wave, and it is a key parameter in the design and operation of wave energy systems.

The energy density of a wave is defined as the energy per unit area of the wave. It is typically expressed in units of joules per square meter (J/m). The energy density of a wave is a function of the wave's amplitude, frequency, and wavelength. It is also a function of the wave's propagation direction and the medium in which the wave is propagating.

The energy density of a wave can be calculated using the wave's amplitude and frequency. The energy density `E` of a wave is given by the equation:

$$
E = \frac{1}{2} \rho g A^2
$$

where `` is the density of the medium, `g` is the acceleration due to gravity, and `A` is the amplitude of the wave. This equation is derived from the equation for the potential energy of a wave, which is given by:

$$
E = \frac{1}{2} \rho g A^2
$$

The energy density of a wave is a critical parameter in the design and operation of wave energy systems. It is used to determine the power output of wave energy converters, which are devices that convert wave energy into electrical power. The power output of a wave energy converter is given by the equation:

$$
P = \frac{1}{2} \rho g A^2 \sigma
$$

where `` is the surface tension of the medium. This equation shows that the power output of a wave energy converter is directly proportional to the energy density of the wave. Therefore, maximizing the energy density of the wave is crucial for maximizing the power output of the wave energy converter.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of wave energy flux, which is another important parameter in the study of ocean waves and their interaction with ships and offshore energy systems.

#### 4.2b Calculation of Wave Energy Density

The calculation of wave energy density involves the application of the principles of fluid dynamics and wave propagation. As we have seen in the previous section, the energy density of a wave is given by the equation:

$$
E = \frac{1}{2} \rho g A^2
$$

where `` is the density of the medium, `g` is the acceleration due to gravity, and `A` is the amplitude of the wave. However, in order to calculate the energy density of a wave, we need to know the wave's amplitude and frequency.

The amplitude of a wave can be determined from its surface elevation. The surface elevation of a wave is given by the equation:

$$
\eta = A \cos(\omega t - kx)
$$

where `` is the surface elevation, `A` is the amplitude of the wave, `` is the angular frequency of the wave, and `k` is the wave number. The angular frequency and wave number are related by the dispersion relation, which is given by:

$$
\omega = \sqrt{gk}
$$

The frequency of a wave can be determined from its period. The period of a wave is given by the equation:

$$
T = \frac{2\pi}{\omega}
$$

Therefore, the energy density of a wave can be calculated using the equations:

$$
E = \frac{1}{2} \rho g A^2 = \frac{1}{2} \rho g \left(A \cos(\omega t - kx)\right)^2
$$

$$
E = \frac{1}{2} \rho g A^2 = \frac{1}{2} \rho g \left(A \cos(\frac{2\pi}{\omega} t - kx)\right)^2
$$

These equations show that the energy density of a wave is a function of the wave's amplitude, frequency, and wavelength. It is also a function of the wave's propagation direction and the medium in which the wave is propagating.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of wave energy flux, which is another important parameter in the study of ocean waves and their interaction with ships and offshore energy systems.

#### 4.2c Wave Energy Density in Ships and Offshore Systems

In the context of ships and offshore systems, wave energy density plays a crucial role in understanding the interaction between waves and these structures. The energy density of waves can be used to predict the forces exerted by the waves on the structures, which is essential for designing structures that can withstand these forces.

The energy density of waves in the vicinity of a ship or an offshore system can be calculated using the equations derived in the previous section. However, in practice, the calculation can be more complex due to the presence of multiple waves with different amplitudes and frequencies.

The total energy density of waves in the vicinity of a ship or an offshore system can be calculated by summing the energy densities of all the individual waves. This can be represented mathematically as:

$$
E_{\text{total}} = \sum_{i=1}^{n} E_i
$$

where `E_i` is the energy density of the `i`-th wave, and `n` is the total number of waves.

In the case of offshore systems, the energy density of waves can be affected by the presence of wave energy converters. These devices convert wave energy into electrical power, which can be used to supply electricity to remote locations. The presence of these devices can increase the energy density of waves in the vicinity of the devices.

The energy density of waves can also be affected by the presence of wave energy storage systems. These systems store wave energy for later use, which can be useful in situations where the demand for electricity is not constant. The energy density of waves in the vicinity of these systems can be calculated using the equations derived in the previous section, taking into account the changes in the wave's amplitude and frequency due to the storage process.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of wave energy flux, which is another important parameter in the study of ocean waves and their interaction with ships and offshore systems.




#### 4.2b Measurement of Wave Energy Density

The measurement of wave energy density is a critical aspect of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. It allows us to quantify the energy contained in a wave, which is crucial for the design and operation of wave energy systems.

There are several methods for measuring wave energy density, each with its own advantages and limitations. One of the most common methods is the use of wave energy density meters. These devices measure the energy density of a wave by detecting the displacement of the wave and converting it into an electrical signal. The energy density is then calculated from the amplitude and frequency of the signal.

Another method for measuring wave energy density is through the use of wave energy flux meters. These devices measure the energy flux, which is the rate of energy transfer per unit area. The energy density is then calculated from the energy flux and the wave's propagation direction.

In addition to these methods, wave energy density can also be measured through the use of wave energy spectrum analyzers. These devices measure the energy spectrum of a wave, which is a representation of the energy contained in different frequency bands. The energy density is then calculated from the energy spectrum.

It's important to note that the accuracy of these measurements depends on the accuracy of the sensors and the calibration of the devices. Therefore, regular calibration and maintenance are essential for ensuring accurate measurements.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of wave energy flux and its importance in the study of ocean waves and their interaction with ships and offshore energy systems.

#### 4.2c Wave Energy Density in Ships and Offshore Energy Systems

#### 4.3a Definition of Wave Energy Flux

Wave energy flux, denoted as $E_w$, is a fundamental concept in the study of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. It is defined as the rate of energy transfer per unit area due to the wave motion. The energy flux is a vector quantity, with the direction of the vector indicating the direction of energy propagation and the magnitude representing the rate of energy transfer.

The energy flux can be expressed in terms of the wave energy density $E_w$ and the wave propagation direction $k$. The energy flux $E_w$ is given by the equation:

$$
E_w = \frac{1}{2} \rho g \sigma^2
$$

where $\rho$ is the fluid density, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, and $\sigma$ is the standard deviation of the free-surface elevation. The potential energy $E_p$ is given by the equation:

$$
E_p = \frac{1}{2} \rho g \sigma^2
$$

For irregular waves, the required wave height is the root-mean-square wave height $H_{rms} \approx \sqrt{8} \sigma$, with $\sigma$ the standard deviation of the free-surface elevation. The potential energy is $E_p = \frac{1}{2} \rho g \sigma^2$ and $E_w \approx \rho g \sigma^2$.

The distribution of the undertow velocity over the water depth is a topic of ongoing research. However, it is known that the undertow velocity is influenced by the wave energy flux. The undertow velocity $\bar{u}$ is given by the equation:

$$
\bar{u} = \frac{1}{2} \rho g \sigma^2
$$

where $\rho$ is the fluid density, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, and $\sigma$ is the standard deviation of the free-surface elevation. The positive flow direction of $\bar{u}$ is in the wave propagation direction.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the concept of wave energy flux and its implications for ship design and offshore energy systems.

#### 4.3b Calculation of Wave Energy Flux

The calculation of wave energy flux involves the application of the principles of fluid dynamics and wave propagation. As we have seen, the energy flux $E_w$ is given by the equation:

$$
E_w = \frac{1}{2} \rho g \sigma^2
$$

where $\rho$ is the fluid density, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, and $\sigma$ is the standard deviation of the free-surface elevation. The standard deviation $\sigma$ can be calculated from the wave height $H$ and the wave period $T$ using the equation:

$$
\sigma = \frac{H}{2\sqrt{3}} \exp \left( \frac{-kz}{\sqrt{3}} \right)
$$

where $k$ is the wave number and $z$ is the vertical coordinate (positive upwards). The wave number $k$ can be calculated from the wave period $T$ and the wave speed $c$ using the equation:

$$
k = \frac{2\pi}{T} \frac{1}{c}
$$

The wave speed $c$ can be calculated from the wave height $H$ and the wave period $T$ using the equation:

$$
c = \sqrt{\frac{gH}{2\pi T}}
$$

The wave energy density $E_w$ can be calculated from the wave energy flux $E_w$ and the wave propagation direction $k$ using the equation:

$$
E_w = \frac{1}{2} \rho g \sigma^2
$$

The wave energy density $E_w$ can also be calculated from the wave height $H$ and the wave period $T$ using the equation:

$$
E_w = \frac{1}{2} \rho g \sigma^2
$$

E_w = \frac{1}{2} \rho g \sigma^2

#### 4.3b Measurement of Wave Energy Flux

The measurement of wave energy flux is a critical aspect of understanding and predicting the behavior of ocean waves. It involves the use of various instruments and techniques, including wave radar, microwave rangefinders, and marine navigation radars.

##### Wave Radar

Wave radar, a type of radar technology, is used to measure waves and water level, including tides. The Miros SM-094, for instance, is a wave radar designed for this purpose. It is used as an air gap (bridge clearance) sensor in NOAA's PORTS system. The WaveRadar REX, on the other hand, is a derivative of a Rosemount tank radar.

The operation of wave radar involves the use of a microwave beam that is directed into the water column. The reflected signal is then analyzed to determine the wave height and direction. This information can be used to calculate the wave energy flux.

##### Microwave Rangefinders

Microwave rangefinders, operating in the vertical mode at GHz frequencies, are another tool for measuring wave energy flux. These instruments are not as affected by fog and water spray as laser rangefinders. They use a continuous wave frequency modulated (CWFM) or pulsed radar waveform to provide range resolution.

The linear size of the footprint of a microwave rangefinder is directly proportional to range, while the area of the footprint is proportional to the square of range. This allows for the calculation of the wave energy flux.

##### Marine Navigation Radars

Marine navigation radars, operating in low grazing angle mode, provide sea clutter images that contain a pattern resembling a sea wave pattern. By digitizing the radar video signal and processing it with a digital computer, sea surface parameters can be calculated. These parameters can then be used to calculate the wave energy flux.

In conclusion, the measurement of wave energy flux is a complex process that involves the use of various instruments and techniques. These measurements are crucial for understanding and predicting the behavior of ocean waves, and are essential for the design and operation of ships and offshore energy systems.

#### 4.3c Wave Energy Flux in Ships

The interaction of waves with ships is a critical aspect of ship design and operation. The wave energy flux, or the rate of energy transfer per unit area due to wave motion, plays a significant role in this interaction. It is a key factor in determining the wave-induced forces and motions of a ship, which can have significant implications for the ship's safety and performance.

##### Wave Energy Flux and Ship Design

The design of a ship is influenced by a variety of factors, including the wave energy flux. The wave energy flux can be used to calculate the wave-induced forces and motions of a ship, which can then be used to optimize the ship's design. For example, the wave energy flux can be used to determine the optimal hull shape for a given set of operating conditions.

The wave energy flux can also be used to predict the behavior of a ship in waves. This is particularly important for ships operating in harsh environments, where the wave energy flux can be high. By understanding the wave energy flux, designers can predict the wave-induced forces and motions of a ship, and design the ship to withstand these forces.

##### Wave Energy Flux and Ship Operation

The operation of a ship is also influenced by the wave energy flux. The wave energy flux can affect the ship's performance, stability, and safety. For example, high wave energy fluxes can lead to increased wave-induced forces and motions, which can affect the ship's stability and safety.

The wave energy flux can also affect the ship's fuel efficiency. High wave energy fluxes can lead to increased wave-induced forces and motions, which can increase the ship's fuel consumption. By understanding the wave energy flux, operators can optimize the ship's operation to minimize fuel consumption and maximize performance.

##### Measurement of Wave Energy Flux in Ships

The measurement of wave energy flux in ships is a complex process that involves the use of various instruments and techniques. These include wave radar, microwave rangefinders, and marine navigation radars, as discussed in the previous section.

The wave energy flux can be calculated from these measurements using the principles of fluid dynamics and wave propagation. This involves the calculation of the wave energy density, which is the energy per unit area due to the wave motion, and the wave energy flux, which is the rate of energy transfer per unit area due to the wave motion.

In conclusion, the wave energy flux plays a crucial role in the design and operation of ships. By understanding and predicting the wave energy flux, designers and operators can optimize the ship's design and operation to maximize performance and safety.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the complex world of wave energy density and flux, two fundamental concepts in the study of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. We have explored the mathematical models that describe these phenomena, and how they are used to predict and understand the behavior of waves in the ocean.

The concept of wave energy density, represented by the equation `$\rho g \frac{H^2}{2}$`, where `$\rho$` is the density of the fluid, `$g$` is the acceleration due to gravity, and `$H$` is the wave height, provides a measure of the energy contained in a wave. This energy density is crucial in understanding the interaction of waves with ships and offshore energy systems.

The wave energy flux, represented by the equation `$\frac{\partial}{\partial x} \left(\rho g \frac{H^2}{2}\right)$`, where `$\frac{\partial}{\partial x}$` is the partial derivative with respect to x, is a measure of the rate of energy transfer in a wave. This concept is fundamental in understanding the propagation of waves in the ocean.

By understanding these concepts and their mathematical representations, we can better predict and understand the behavior of waves in the ocean, and how they interact with ships and offshore energy systems. This knowledge is crucial in the design and operation of these systems, and in ensuring their safety and efficiency.

## Chapter: Wave-Structure Interaction

### Introduction

The interaction of waves with structures is a critical aspect of ocean engineering. This chapter, "Wave-Structure Interaction," delves into the complex dynamics of this interaction, exploring the fundamental principles and mathematical models that govern it. 

Wave-structure interaction is a multifaceted phenomenon that plays a pivotal role in the design and operation of various marine structures, including ships, offshore platforms, and coastal defenses. Understanding this interaction is crucial for predicting the behavior of these structures under wave loading, which is essential for their safe and efficient operation.

In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental principles that govern wave-structure interaction, including the principles of wave propagation, wave reflection, and wave absorption. We will also delve into the mathematical models that describe these phenomena, including the wave equation and the boundary element method.

We will also discuss the practical implications of wave-structure interaction, including its impact on the design and operation of various marine structures. This will include a discussion of the challenges posed by wave-structure interaction, as well as strategies for mitigating these challenges.

This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive guide to wave-structure interaction, equipping readers with the knowledge and tools they need to understand and predict this complex phenomenon. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a practicing engineer, we hope that this chapter will serve as a valuable resource in your exploration of wave-structure interaction.




#### 4.3c Effects of Wave Energy Flux on Wave Dynamics

The wave energy flux plays a crucial role in the dynamics of ocean waves. It is the rate at which wave energy is transferred across a given area. The effects of wave energy flux on wave dynamics are multifaceted and complex, involving interactions with the ocean floor, the atmosphere, and other waves.

##### Interaction with the Ocean Floor

The interaction of waves with the ocean floor is a key factor in wave dynamics. When waves break on the ocean floor, they transfer energy to the floor, which can cause changes in the wave's direction and intensity. This interaction is particularly important in shallow water, where the wave's wavelength is comparable to or smaller than the water depth.

The wave energy flux can be used to calculate the rate of energy transfer to the ocean floor. This can be done using the wave energy density, which is the amount of wave energy per unit area. The wave energy flux is then given by the product of the wave energy density and the wave's propagation speed.

##### Interaction with the Atmosphere

The interaction of waves with the atmosphere is another important aspect of wave dynamics. This interaction can lead to the generation of new waves, the transfer of energy between different waves, and the dissipation of wave energy.

The wave energy flux can be used to calculate the rate of energy transfer to the atmosphere. This can be done using the wave energy density and the wave's propagation speed, similar to the calculation for the ocean floor.

##### Interaction with Other Waves

Waves can also interact with each other, leading to phenomena such as wave interference and wave breaking. These interactions can be influenced by the wave energy flux.

The wave energy flux can be used to calculate the rate of energy transfer between waves. This can be done using the wave energy density and the wave's propagation speed, similar to the calculations for the ocean floor and the atmosphere.

In conclusion, the wave energy flux plays a crucial role in the dynamics of ocean waves. It is involved in the interaction of waves with the ocean floor, the atmosphere, and other waves. Understanding the effects of wave energy flux on wave dynamics is essential for predicting and managing the behavior of ocean waves.

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will delve into the concept of wave momentum flux, a crucial aspect of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. Wave momentum flux is a fundamental concept in the study of wave dynamics, and it plays a significant role in the design and operation of ships and offshore energy systems.

The momentum flux of a wave is a measure of the momentum transfer per unit area due to the wave. It is a vector quantity, with both magnitude and direction. The direction of the momentum flux vector is typically taken to be the direction of propagation of the wave. The magnitude of the momentum flux vector is proportional to the square of the wave amplitude and the wave frequency.

In the context of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems, the wave momentum flux is of particular interest. The interaction of waves with these systems can lead to significant momentum transfer, which can have significant implications for the performance and stability of the systems.

In this chapter, we will explore the mathematical formulation of wave momentum flux, including the derivation of the wave momentum flux vector. We will also discuss the physical interpretation of the wave momentum flux vector and its implications for ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems.

We will also delve into the practical applications of wave momentum flux in the design and operation of ships and offshore energy systems. This includes the use of wave momentum flux in the design of wave-resistant structures, the prediction of wave-induced forces on offshore energy systems, and the optimization of ship performance in wave conditions.

By the end of this chapter, readers should have a comprehensive understanding of wave momentum flux and its role in ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. This knowledge will provide a solid foundation for the subsequent chapters, which will delve deeper into the practical aspects of wave-structure interaction and wave-energy conversion.

# Cauchy momentum equation

## Differential derivation

Let us start with the generalized momentum conservation principle which can be written as follows: "The change in system momentum is proportional to the resulting force acting on this system". It is expressed by the formula:

<math display="block">\vec p(t+\Delta t) - \vec p(t) = \Delta t \vec\bar F</math>

where <math>\vec p(t)</math> is momentum in time `t`, <math>\vec\bar F</math> is force averaged over <math>\Delta t</math>. After dividing by <math>\Delta t</math> and passing to the limit <math>\Delta t \to 0</math> we get (derivative):

<math display="block">\frac{d\vec p}{dt} = \vec F</math>

Let us analyse each side of the equation above.

### Right side

We split the forces into body forces <math>\vec F_m</math> and surface forces <math>\vec F_p</math>

<math display="block">\vec F=\vec F_p + \vec F_m</math>

Surface forces act on walls of the cubic fluid element. For each wall, the "X" component of these forces was marked in the figure with a cubic element (in the form of a product of stress and surface area e.g. <math>-\sigma_{xx} \, dy \, dz</math> with units <math display="inline">\mathrm{Pa\cdot m\cdot m = \frac{N}{m^2} \cdot m^2 = N}</math>).

Adding forces (their "X" components) acting on each of the cube walls, we get:

<math display="block">F_p^x =
\left(\sigma_{xx}+\frac{\partial\sigma_{xx}}{\partial x}dx\right)dy\,dz
-\sigma_{xx}dy\,dz
+\left(\sigma_{yx}+\frac{\partial\sigma_{yx}}{\partial y}dy\right)dx\,dz
-\sigma_{yx}dx\,dz
+\left(\sigma_{zx}+\frac{\partial\sigma_{zx}}{\partial z}dz\right)dx\,dy
-\sigma_{zx}dx\,dy
</math>

After ordering <math>F_p^x</math> and performing similar reasoning for components <math>F_p^y, F_p^z</math> (they have not been shown in the figure, but these would be vectors parallel to the Y and Z axes, respectively) we get:

F_p^x &= \frac{\partial\sigma_{xx}}{\partial x}\,dx\,dy\,dz
+ \frac{\partial\sigma_{yx}}{\partial y}\,dy\,dx\,dz
+ \frac{\partial\sigma_{zx}}{\partial z}\,dz\,dx\,dy
\\[0.8em]
F_p^y &= \frac{\partial\sigma_{xy}}{\partial x}\,dx\,dy\,dz
+ \frac{\partial\sigma_{yy}}{\partial y}\,dy\,dx\,dz
+ \frac{\partial\sigma_{zy}}{\partial z}\,dz\,dx\,dy
\\[0.8em]
F_p^z &= \frac{\partial\sigma_{xz}}{\partial x}\,dx\,dy\,dz
+ \frac{\partial\sigma_{yz}}{\partial y}\,dy\,dx\,dz
+ \frac{\partial\sigma_{zz}}{\partial z}\,dz\,dx\,dy
</math>

The left side of the equation is the change in momentum, which can be expressed as:

<math display="block">\frac{d\vec p}{dt} = \vec F</math>

This equation represents the principle of momentum conservation for waves. It states that the change in momentum of a system is equal to the resultant force acting on the system. This principle is fundamental to understanding the interaction of waves with ships and offshore energy systems.

# Cauchy momentum equation

## Differential derivation

Let us start with the generalized momentum conservation principle which can be written as follows: "The change in system momentum is proportional to the resulting force acting on this system". It is expressed by the formula:

<math display="block">\vec p(t+\Delta t) - \vec p(t) = \Delta t \vec\bar F</math>

where <math>\vec p(t)</math> is momentum in time `t`, <math>\vec\bar F</math> is force averaged over <math>\Delta t</math>. After dividing by <math>\Delta t</math> and passing to the limit <math>\Delta t \to 0</math> we get (derivative):

<math display="block">\frac{d\vec p}{dt} = \vec F</math>

Let us analyse each side of the equation above.

### Right side

We split the forces into body forces <math>\vec F_m</math> and surface forces <math>\vec F_p</math>

<math display="block">\vec F=\vec F_p + \vec F_m</math>

Surface forces act on walls of the cubic fluid element. For each wall, the "X" component of these forces was marked in the figure with a cubic element (in the form of a product of stress and surface area e.g. <math>-\sigma_{xx} \, dy \, dz</math> with units <math display="inline">\mathrm{Pa\cdot m\cdot m = \frac{N}{m^2} \cdot m^2 = N}</math>).

Adding forces (their "X" components) acting on each of the cube walls, we get:

<math display="block">F_p^x =
\left(\sigma_{xx}+\frac{\partial\sigma_{xx}}{\partial x}dx\right)dy\,dz
-\sigma_{xx}dy\,dz
+\left(\sigma_{yx}+\frac{\partial\sigma_{yx}}{\partial y}dy\right)dx\,dz
-\sigma_{yx}dx\,dz
+\left(\sigma_{zx}+\frac{\partial\sigma_{zx}}{\partial z}dz\right)dx\,dy
-\sigma_{zx}dx\,dy
</math>

After ordering <math>F_p^x</math> and performing similar reasoning for components <math>F_p^y, F_p^z</math> (they have not been shown in the figure, but these would be vectors parallel to the Y and Z axes, respectively) we get:

F_p^x &= \frac{\partial\sigma_{xx}}{\partial x}\,dx\,dy\,dz
+ \frac{\partial\sigma_{yx}}{\partial y}\,dy\,dx\,dz
+ \frac{\partial\sigma_{zx}}{\partial z}\,dz\,dx\,dy
\\[0.8em]
F_p^y &= \frac{\partial\sigma_{xy}}{\partial x}\,dx\,dy\,dz
+ \frac{\partial\sigma_{yy}}{\partial y}\,dy\,dx\,dz
+ \frac{\partial\sigma_{zy}}{\partial z}\,dz\,dx\,dy
\\[0.8em]
F_p^z &= \frac{\partial\sigma_{xz}}{\partial x}\,dx\,dy\,dz
+ \frac{\partial\sigma_{yz}}{\partial y}\,dy\,dx\,dz
+ \frac{\partial\sigma_{zz}}{\partial z}\,dz\,dx\,dy
</math>

### Left side

The left side of the equation represents the change in momentum of the fluid element. This can be calculated by taking the derivative of the momentum vector with respect to time.

<math display="block">\frac{d\vec p}{dt} = \frac{d}{dt}\left(\vec p_0 + \vec p_1\right) = \frac{d\vec p_0}{dt} + \frac{d\vec p_1}{dt}</math>

where <math>\vec p_0</math> is the momentum of the fluid element at the initial time <math>t_0</math> and <math>\vec p_1</math> is the momentum at the final time <math>t_1</math>.

### Conclusion

In conclusion, the Cauchy momentum equation is a powerful tool for analyzing the forces acting on a fluid element. By understanding the right and left sides of the equation, we can gain insight into the behavior of the fluid element and its interaction with surrounding forces. This equation is a fundamental concept in fluid dynamics and is essential for understanding more complex phenomena such as wave momentum flux.

#### 5.2a Definition of Wave Momentum Density

Wave momentum density is a fundamental concept in the study of ocean waves and their interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. It is defined as the rate of change of momentum of a fluid element as it moves through a control volume. In the context of ocean waves, the momentum density is a measure of the force exerted by the wave on a structure.

The momentum density of a wave is a vector quantity, with components in the x, y, and z directions. The x and y components represent the horizontal momentum density, while the z component represents the vertical momentum density. The horizontal momentum density is further divided into two components: the mean momentum density and the oscillatory momentum density.

The mean momentum density, denoted as $M_{m}$, is the average momentum density over a wave cycle. It is a measure of the average force exerted by the wave on a structure. The oscillatory momentum density, denoted as $M_{o}$, is the variation in momentum density over a wave cycle. It is a measure of the fluctuating force exerted by the wave on a structure.

The total momentum density, $M$, is the sum of the mean and oscillatory momentum densities. It is a measure of the total force exerted by the wave on a structure. The total momentum density can be expressed as:

$$
M = M_{m} + M_{o}
$$

The momentum density is a crucial concept in the study of wave-structure interaction. It provides a quantitative measure of the forces exerted by waves on a structure, which is essential for the design and analysis of ships and offshore energy systems. By understanding the momentum density, engineers and researchers can predict the forces exerted by waves on a structure and design structures that can withstand these forces.

In the next section, we will explore the factors that influence the momentum density and how it can be calculated. We will also discuss the practical applications of momentum density in the design and operation of ships and offshore energy systems.

#### 5.2b Calculation of Wave Momentum Density

The calculation of wave momentum density involves the integration of the wave momentum flux over the wave's wavelength and depth. The wave momentum flux, $M_f$, is defined as the product of the wave momentum density and the wave's wavelength and depth. It is given by the equation:

$$
M_f = M \cdot \lambda \cdot \delta
$$

where $M$ is the wave momentum density, $\lambda$ is the wave's wavelength, and $\delta$ is the wave's depth.

The wave momentum density, $M$, is a function of the wave's amplitude, frequency, and direction. It can be calculated using the wave's orbital velocity, $U$, and the wave's potential energy density, $E_p$, as follows:

$$
M = \rho \cdot U \cdot E_p
$$

where $\rho$ is the density of the fluid.

The wave's orbital velocity, $U$, is a function of the wave's amplitude and frequency. It can be calculated using the wave's phase speed, $c$, and the wave's wavelength, $\lambda$, as follows:

$$
U = c \cdot \lambda
$$

The wave's potential energy density, $E_p$, is a function of the wave's amplitude and frequency. It can be calculated using the wave's kinetic energy density, $E_k$, and the wave's potential energy density, $E_p$, as follows:

$$
E_p = E_k + E_p
$$

where $E_k$ is the wave's kinetic energy density, and $E_p$ is the wave's potential energy density.

The wave's kinetic energy density, $E_k$, is a function of the wave's amplitude and frequency. It can be calculated using the wave's orbital velocity, $U$, and the wave's kinetic energy density, $E_k$, as follows:

$$
E_k = \frac{1}{2} \cdot \rho \cdot U^2
$$

The wave's potential energy density, $E_p$, is a function of the wave's amplitude and frequency. It can be calculated using the wave's potential energy density, $E_p$, and the wave's potential energy density, $E_p$, as follows:

$$
E_p = \frac{1}{2} \cdot \rho \cdot g \cdot \eta^2
$$

where $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, and $\eta$ is the wave's amplitude.

By integrating the wave momentum flux over the wave's wavelength and depth, the total wave momentum density can be calculated. This calculation is crucial for understanding the forces exerted by waves on a structure and for designing structures that can withstand these forces.

#### 5.2c Wave Momentum Density in Ships and Offshore Energy Systems

In the context of ships and offshore energy systems, the calculation of wave momentum density is crucial for understanding the forces exerted by waves on these structures. The wave momentum density, $M$, is a function of the wave's amplitude, frequency, and direction. It can be calculated using the wave's orbital velocity, $U$, and the wave's potential energy density, $E_p$, as follows:

The wave's orbital velocity, $U$, is a function of the wave's amplitude and frequency. It can be calculated using the wave's phase speed, $c$, and the wave's wavelength, $\lambda$, as follows:

$$

The wave's potential energy density, $E_p$, is a function of the wave's amplitude and frequency. It can be calculated using the wave's kinetic energy density, $E_k$, and the wave's potential energy density, $E_p$, as follows:

$$
E_p = E_k + E_p
$$

where $E_k$ is the wave's kinetic energy density, and $E_p$ is the wave's potential energy density.

The wave's kinetic energy density, $E_k$, is a function of the wave's amplitude and frequency. It can be calculated using the wave's orbital velocity, $U$, and the wave's kinetic energy density, $E_k$, as follows:

$$

By integrating the wave momentum flux over the wave's wavelength and depth, the total wave momentum density can be calculated. This calculation is crucial for understanding the forces exerted by waves on a structure and for designing structures that can withstand these forces.

## Chapter: Wave Momentum Dissipation

### Introduction

The study of wave momentum dissipation is a critical aspect of understanding the interaction of waves with ships and offshore energy systems. This chapter will delve into the fundamental principles and mechanisms that govern the dissipation of wave momentum. 

Wave momentum dissipation refers to the process by which the momentum of a wave is converted into other forms of energy, such as heat or turbulence, as the wave propagates through a medium. This process is crucial in the context of ocean engineering, as it influences the design and operation of ships and offshore energy systems. 

The chapter will explore the mathematical models that describe wave momentum dissipation, including the wave momentum dissipation function and the wave momentum dissipation rate. These models will be presented in a clear and accessible manner, using the popular Markdown format and the MathJax library for rendering mathematical expressions. 

The chapter will also discuss the practical implications of wave momentum dissipation, including its effects on wave propagation and its role in the design of ships and offshore energy systems. Real-world examples and case studies will be used to illustrate these concepts, providing a comprehensive understanding of wave momentum dissipation in the context of ocean engineering.

By the end of this chapter, readers should have a solid understanding of wave momentum dissipation and its importance in the study of wave-structure interaction. This knowledge will be invaluable for anyone involved in the design, operation, or study of ships or offshore energy systems.




#### 5.2b Measurement of Wave Momentum Density

The measurement of wave momentum density is a critical aspect of understanding wave-structure interaction. It involves the use of various instruments and techniques to measure the momentum density of waves. One such instrument is the Deep-ocean Assessment and Reporting of Tsunamis (DART) buoy system.

The DART buoy system is made up of three parts: a bottom pressure recorder (BPR), a moored surface buoy, and a data transmission link between the two. The BPR, anchored to the bottom of the sea floor, measures the pressure of the surrounding sea water. The moored surface buoy, connected to the BPR via an acoustic transmission link, sends this data to the surface by radio. The data is then transmitted to satellites and ground stations for further processing.

The data sent from the BPR to the surface buoy consists of the temperature and pressure of the surrounding sea water. This data is used to calculate the height of the waves surrounding the buoy. The temperature of the surrounding sea water is important because it affects the water's density, thus the pressure, and therefore the sea temperature is required to accurately measure the height of the ocean swells.

The DART buoy system provides a high-resolution measurement of the pressure of the surrounding sea water, which can be used to calculate the momentum density of the waves. The system is designed to retrieve and release data every 15 seconds to get an average reading of the current weather conditions. This allows for a very stable, long lived, very high resolution pressure sensor, which is a critical enabling technology for the DART system.

The DART buoy system is an example of a sophisticated instrument used to measure wave momentum density. Other techniques, such as wave tank experiments and numerical simulations, can also be used to measure wave momentum density. These techniques allow for a more detailed analysis of the wave momentum density, including its components in the x, y, and z directions, and its variation over a wave cycle.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the calculation of wave momentum density, including the factors that influence it and how it can be calculated from the data collected by the DART buoy system and other instruments.

#### 5.2c Wave Momentum Density in Ships and Offshore Energy Systems

The wave momentum density plays a crucial role in the design and operation of ships and offshore energy systems. It is a key factor in determining the forces exerted by waves on these systems, which can significantly impact their performance and safety.

In the context of ships, the wave momentum density is particularly important in the design of hulls and propulsion systems. The hull of a ship is designed to withstand the forces exerted by waves, and the wave momentum density is a key factor in determining these forces. Similarly, the propulsion system of a ship must be able to overcome the wave momentum density to move the ship through the water.

In offshore energy systems, the wave momentum density is a critical factor in the design of foundations and mooring systems. The foundations of these systems must be able to withstand the forces exerted by waves, and the wave momentum density is a key factor in determining these forces. Similarly, the mooring systems of these systems must be able to withstand the forces exerted by waves, and the wave momentum density is a key factor in determining these forces.

The wave momentum density can be calculated from the data collected by the DART buoy system and other instruments. This allows for a detailed analysis of the wave momentum density, including its components in the x, y, and z directions, and its variation over a wave cycle. This information can then be used to design and optimize ships and offshore energy systems for maximum performance and safety.

#### 5.2c Effects of Wave Momentum Density on Wave Dynamics

The wave momentum density plays a crucial role in the dynamics of ocean waves. It is a measure of the momentum of the wave per unit area, and it is a key factor in determining the wave's propagation and interaction with structures. In this section, we will explore the effects of wave momentum density on wave dynamics.

#### 5.2c.1 Wave Propagation

The wave momentum density is directly related to the wave's propagation. As a wave propagates, it carries a certain amount of momentum with it. This momentum is represented by the wave momentum density. The higher the wave momentum density, the more momentum the wave carries, and the faster it can propagate.

The wave momentum density is also related to the wave's energy. The wave's energy is proportional to the square of its momentum density. This relationship is known as the wave energy density relationship. It is given by the equation:

$$
E = \frac{1}{2} \rho g \left( \frac{H}{2} \right)^2
$$

where $E$ is the wave energy density, $\rho$ is the density of the water, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, and $H$ is the wave height.

#### 5.2c.2 Wave Interaction with Structures

The wave momentum density also plays a crucial role in the interaction of waves with structures. When a wave interacts with a structure, it can cause the structure to move. This movement is a result of the wave's momentum being transferred to the structure. The amount of momentum transferred is determined by the wave momentum density.

The wave momentum density is also related to the wave's force on a structure. The wave's force on a structure is proportional to the wave momentum density. This relationship is known as the wave force relationship. It is given by the equation:

$$
F = \rho g \frac{H}{2} \frac{dA}{dx}
$$

where $F$ is the wave force, $\rho$ is the density of the water, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, $H$ is the wave height, $dA/dx$ is the change in wave area with respect to distance.

#### 5.2c.3 Wave Breaking

The wave momentum density can also affect the breaking of waves. When a wave breaks, it loses its momentum and energy. The amount of momentum and energy lost is determined by the wave momentum density. The higher the wave momentum density, the more momentum and energy the wave can lose before breaking.

In conclusion, the wave momentum density plays a crucial role in the dynamics of ocean waves. It affects the wave's propagation, interaction with structures, and breaking. Understanding the effects of wave momentum density on wave dynamics is essential for predicting and mitigating the impact of waves on structures and the environment.




#### 5.3a Definition of Wave Momentum Flux

The wave momentum flux is a fundamental concept in the study of ocean waves and their interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. It is defined as the rate of change of wave momentum with respect to the wave's direction of propagation. In other words, it is the force exerted by the wave on a unit area perpendicular to its direction of propagation.

The wave momentum flux, denoted as $F$, is given by the equation:

$$
F = \rho g \frac{H}{2} \frac{dA}{dx}
$$

where $\rho$ is the density of the water, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, $H$ is the wave height, $dA/dx$ is the change in wave area with respect to the wave's direction of propagation.

The wave momentum flux is a vector quantity, with both magnitude and direction. Its magnitude represents the force exerted by the wave, while its direction is perpendicular to the wave's direction of propagation.

The wave momentum flux plays a crucial role in the dynamics of ocean waves. It is responsible for the wave's propagation, as it is the force that drives the wave forward. It also plays a key role in the wave's interaction with structures, as it is the force that causes the structure to move when it interacts with the wave.

In the next sections, we will explore the effects of wave momentum flux on wave dynamics and its role in wave interaction with structures in more detail.

#### 5.3b Calculation of Wave Momentum Flux

The calculation of wave momentum flux involves the use of the wave momentum flux equation, which is given by:

$$
F = \rho g \frac{H}{2} \frac{dA}{dx}
$$

This equation can be used to calculate the wave momentum flux for a given wave, provided that the wave's density, height, and change in area with respect to its direction of propagation are known.

The density of the water, $\rho$, can be measured directly using a density meter. Alternatively, it can be calculated using the equation of state for seawater, which relates the density of seawater to its salinity, temperature, and pressure.

The wave height, $H$, can be measured directly using a wave height sensor. Alternatively, it can be calculated from the wave's spectral density, which is the power spectrum of the wave's amplitude.

The change in wave area with respect to its direction of propagation, $dA/dx$, can be calculated from the wave's spectral density. This involves integrating the spectral density over the wave's direction of propagation.

Once the wave momentum flux, $F$, has been calculated, it can be used to determine the wave's force on a structure. This is done by multiplying the wave momentum flux by the area of the structure perpendicular to the wave's direction of propagation.

In the next section, we will explore the effects of wave momentum flux on wave dynamics and its role in wave interaction with structures in more detail.

#### 5.3c Wave Momentum Flux in Wave Interaction

The wave momentum flux plays a crucial role in the interaction between waves and structures. As we have seen in the previous section, the wave momentum flux, $F$, is given by:

$$
F = \rho g \frac{H}{2} \frac{dA}{dx}
$$

This equation can be used to calculate the wave's force on a structure, which is given by the product of the wave momentum flux and the area of the structure perpendicular to the wave's direction of propagation.

The wave momentum flux is responsible for the transfer of momentum from the wave to the structure. This transfer of momentum can cause the structure to move, which can have significant implications for the design and operation of offshore energy systems.

For example, in the case of a wave hitting a ship, the wave momentum flux can cause the ship to move. This movement can be calculated using the equation of motion for a ship in waves, which is given by:

$$
F = m \frac{d^2x}{dt^2}
$$

where $F$ is the wave momentum flux, $m$ is the mass of the ship, and $\frac{d^2x}{dt^2}$ is the acceleration of the ship.

By combining these equations, we can determine the acceleration of the ship due to the wave momentum flux. This can be used to calculate the ship's displacement, which is the distance the ship moves due to the wave.

In the next section, we will explore the effects of wave momentum flux on wave dynamics and its role in wave interaction with structures in more detail.




#### 5.3b Measurement of Wave Momentum Flux

The measurement of wave momentum flux is a crucial aspect of studying ocean waves and their interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. It involves the use of various instruments and techniques to measure the wave's density, height, and change in area with respect to its direction of propagation.

One of the most commonly used instruments for measuring wave momentum flux is the scatterometer. A scatterometer, such as the QuikSCAT, emits pulses of low-power microwave radiation and measures the power reflected back to its receiving antenna from the wind-roughened sea surface. The backscatter measurements are obtained at fixed incidence angles of 46 and 54, providing up to four views of each region of the surface at different incidence angles.

The QuikSCAT measurements are processed to yield a spatial resolution of about 25 km. A higher spatial resolution of 12.5 km is also achieved through special processing, but with significantly more measurement noise. An even higher spatial resolution of 5 km is produced, but only for limited regions and special cases.

The <sub>0</sub> observations from the QuikSCAT are calibrated to the wind speed and direction of the wind at a reference height of 10 meters above the sea surface. This calibration allows for the calculation of the wave momentum flux using the wave momentum flux equation:

$$
F = \rho g \frac{H}{2} \frac{dA}{dx}
$$

where $\rho$ is the density of the water, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, $H$ is the wave height, and $dA/dx$ is the change in wave area with respect to its direction of propagation.

In addition to scatterometers, other instruments such as wave radar and wave buoys can also be used to measure wave momentum flux. These instruments use different principles and techniques, but all of them rely on the measurement of the wave's density, height, and change in area with respect to its direction of propagation.

In the next section, we will discuss the effects of wave momentum flux on wave dynamics and its role in wave interaction with structures.

#### 5.3c Wave Momentum Flux in Ships and Offshore Energy Systems

The wave momentum flux plays a significant role in the design and operation of ships and offshore energy systems. It is a key factor in determining the forces exerted by waves on these systems, which can lead to structural failure if not properly accounted for.

In the context of ships, the wave momentum flux can be used to calculate the wave-induced forces on the ship's hull. These forces can be used to design the ship's hull to withstand the wave loads, and to predict the ship's motion in waves. The wave momentum flux can also be used to calculate the wave-induced loads on offshore energy systems, such as wind turbines and wave energy converters.

The wave momentum flux can be calculated using the wave momentum flux equation:

$$
F = \rho g \frac{H}{2} \frac{dA}{dx}
$$

where $\rho$ is the density of the water, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, $H$ is the wave height, and $dA/dx$ is the change in wave area with respect to its direction of propagation.

The wave momentum flux can be measured using various instruments, such as scatterometers, wave radar, and wave buoys. These instruments provide the necessary data to calculate the wave momentum flux, and can be used to validate numerical models of wave-structure interaction.

In the next section, we will discuss the effects of wave momentum flux on the design and operation of ships and offshore energy systems.




#### 5.3c Effects of Wave Momentum Flux on Wave Dynamics

The wave momentum flux plays a crucial role in the dynamics of ocean waves. It is a measure of the momentum transfer between the wave and the surrounding fluid, and it is directly related to the wave's energy and propagation characteristics.

The wave momentum flux can be expressed as:

$$
F = \rho g \frac{H}{2} \frac{dA}{dx}
$$

where $\rho$ is the density of the water, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, $H$ is the wave height, and $dA/dx$ is the change in wave area with respect to its direction of propagation.

The wave momentum flux has several important effects on wave dynamics. These include:

1. **Wave Propagation:** The wave momentum flux is directly related to the wave's propagation characteristics. A higher wave momentum flux indicates a stronger wave, which can propagate faster and over longer distances.

2. **Wave Energy Transfer:** The wave momentum flux is also related to the wave's energy transfer. A higher wave momentum flux indicates a greater energy transfer, which can lead to more significant wave-induced forces on ships and offshore energy systems.

3. **Wave Breaking:** The wave momentum flux can also affect wave breaking. A higher wave momentum flux can lead to more intense wave breaking, which can have significant implications for the wave's energy and propagation characteristics.

4. **Wave Interaction with Ships and Offshore Energy Systems:** The wave momentum flux plays a crucial role in the interaction between waves and ships or offshore energy systems. A higher wave momentum flux can lead to more significant wave-induced forces, which can affect the structural integrity of these systems.

In conclusion, wave momentum flux is a crucial concept in understanding the interaction between ocean waves and ships or offshore energy systems. Its accurate calculation and consideration are essential for ensuring the safety and reliability of these systems in the harsh marine environment.

## Chapter: Ocean Wave Interaction with Ships and Offshore Energy Systems: A Comprehensive Guide

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will explore the topic of wave incidence and reflection, which is a crucial aspect of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. Wave incidence refers to the process of waves approaching a structure or vessel, while wave reflection refers to the deflection of waves away from a structure or vessel. These phenomena play a significant role in the design and operation of ships and offshore energy systems, as they can have a significant impact on the stability and safety of these structures.

The study of wave incidence and reflection is essential for understanding the behavior of ocean waves and their interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. It involves the use of mathematical models and equations to predict the behavior of waves as they approach and interact with these structures. These models take into account various factors such as the geometry of the structure, the properties of the waves, and the environmental conditions.

In this chapter, we will cover the fundamental principles of wave incidence and reflection, including the concepts of wave reflection coefficient and wave incidence angle. We will also discuss the different types of wave incidence and reflection, such as specular and diffuse reflection, and how they affect the behavior of waves. Additionally, we will explore the impact of wave incidence and reflection on the design and operation of ships and offshore energy systems, and how it can be mitigated.

Overall, this chapter aims to provide a comprehensive guide to wave incidence and reflection, equipping readers with the necessary knowledge and tools to understand and analyze the behavior of ocean waves as they interact with ships and offshore energy systems. By the end of this chapter, readers will have a better understanding of the complex dynamics of wave incidence and reflection and its importance in the field of ocean engineering.
